Mapping value chain collaboration opportuni-
ties for circular economy transition
Master Thesis
DTU Civil Engineering

==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The transition to a circular economy requires companies to adopt new collaborative ap-
proaches across the value chain, involving multiple partners in order to implement circular
strategies. By partnering with actors who possess complementary skills, companies can
overcome barriers that would be difficult to solve individually. However, such collabo-
rations require time and resources, especially in the initial stages, directly affecting the
net-value creation of the collaboration itself. This thesis investigates how collaboration
can be supported to enable more circular economy transitions. This is done by identifying
224 barriers, influencing such collaborations, and developing an initial support to map op-
portunities and guide companies in establishing and managing collaborative processes.
Through a systematic literature review and empirical data collection, this thesis found that
data transparency, trust, commitment, and alignment of goals are main areas of focus.
The thesis proposes an initial support concept, offering companies a structured approach
to mapping collaboration goals, identifying opportunities, and enhancing transparency
between partners. The work concludes by identifying areas for further research, includ-
ing broader empirical studies across industries, cross-sector workshops, and longitudinal
studies to track the evolution of circular collaborations over time.

Learning Objectives
Analysis
• Investigate, describe, and present different barriers, risk and failures that may occur
when implementing value chain collaborations for circular economy transition.
• Investigate, describe, and present different collaboration dynamics for CE.
• Define relevant knowledge within the field of CE in relation to linear economy and
sustainability.
Design & Evaluation
• Develop an overview of Opportunities for value chain collaboration for CE transition.
• Develop an initial support that guides the establishment of collaboration in CE tran-
sition.
• Identify future research areas enabling collaboration for CE transition.
Overall Project
• Plan, structure, and execute a long-term project in collaboration with multiple stake-
holders and make requested deliveries.
• Collect and structure information and data from a problem context, using contex-
tual interviews and other appropriate methods, and extract and utilize the relevant
components for an analysis.
• Communicating the analyzed literature in a critical and objective manner.
• Communicate the results of the review to an audience of peers.

Project structure
This project is classified as the Design Research Methodology, design study type 1 [1],
which includes a review-based Research Clarification and a comprehensive Descriptive
Study I involving literature reviews and empirical studies with a qualitative approach. This
process has involved iterations with each cycle giving increasing understanding and rise
to further investigation.
The project has been structured into 6 phases divided by milestones and outcomes.
Figure 1: Project structure. Illustration of the different phases with description for each
phase, and their outcome.

Thesis structure
This thesis consists of five chapters. An overview of the project’s six phases is described
below and additionally illustrated in Figure 1.
Chapter 1
Describes the results from Phase 1 by setting the scene for the project, and introducing
the motivation and context of the research. The chapter ends with identifying the research
gaps and corresponding research questions
Chapter 2
Describes the research methodology and structure used, within this thesis, to answer the
research questions. Finally, it presents the specific methods applied to each research
question.
Chapter 3
Research Clarification: Describes the full work of Phase 1, where research gaps are
identified and research questions are developed. This has been developed through an
exploratory literature review of the area and an earlier investigation.
Chapter 4
Presents the development of the material made for Descriptive Study 1. Describing the
planning, execution, and analysis in Phases 2 & 3:Identification and comparison of support
and mechanisms and Phase 4 & 5: Identification and systematization of opportunities.
.
Chapter 5
Discusses the main findings from the descriptive study as a part of Phase 6, with a focus
on suggesting an initial concept for support to collaboration for circular economy tran-
sition and ending with a focus on the research questions. Discusses the contributions,
implications, and limitations of the process. Followed by the conclusion on the findings,
reflections, and recommendations for future research.

Reading Guide
Nomenclature
Actor
An actor is a term covering any stakeholder, partner, company, NGO in this thesis.
Barrier
A barrier can be a challenge, constraint, obstacle, issue, problem, or other similar requiring
resources to overcome. In this thesis a barrier doesn’t have to be the stopping factor, it
can also be overcome; however, it is a resource-expensive obstacle or a bottleneck.
Capabilities
With capabilities, there is a focus on the specific resources of abilities in an organization,
e.g. knowledge, skills, power, or experience. Capabilities can, in collaboration between
two actors, be developed together, such as risk management, communication, data col-
lection, etc.
Collaboration
Collaboration is an overarching term that encompasses partnership, cooperation, coordi-
nation, or co-creation. In this thesis, collaboration is defined as a situation in which two or
more actors use resources for development, innovation, or operations, without expecting
direct payment for the resource, with the goal of achieving a higher net value creation
than if they were working independently.
Ecosystem
An ecosystem refers to a multi-actor system with a shared goal, such as a specific sector
or circular economy business. The participants can include industry players, municipali-
ties, universities, NGOs, and consumers.
Opportunity
When you know how to overcome a barrier, it becomes an opportunity. Understanding a
barrier includes recognizing challenges, obstacles, issues, or a resource-intensive task.
Resource
In this thesis, the term ”resource” encompasses all the value that a company can con-
tribute in a collaboration, and is an expense for the company itself. This value can include
knowledge, time, skills, experience, power, money, energy, or materials.
Support
A ”support” is an overall term in this thesis that describes what could be a guide, frame-
work, or tool.
Transition
In this thesis, transition refers to the process of shifting from a linear economy to a circular
economy, requiring changes in business models, value creation, and collaboration across
the value chain.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
1
==PAGE:18==
1
Chapter: Introduction
1.1
Introduction and Motivation
There is an increased focus on including social and environmental benefits to companies’
bottom line, leading to interest in circular economy implementation. The transition from
linear to circular economy requires new capabilities, resources, and collaboration between
companies for the implementation of circular strategies.
By partnering with actors who possess complementary skills, companies can overcome
barriers that would be difficult to solve individually. However, such collaborations require
time and resources, especially in the initial stages, directly affecting the net-value creation
of the collaboration itself.
Despite the recognized importance of collaboration for circular transitions, there is a lack
of generic frameworks to guide the implementation of CE principles in a way that is useful
for all partners in the value chain. The current approaches often leave the responsibility
for driving collaboration with the OEM or product manufacturer in the value chain.
This thesis is motivated by the need for a shared support that all partners can use to
compare their positions, identify gaps, and clarify needs when collaborating for circular
economy transition. By following the same process, partners can quickly identify deal
breakers and critical issues that must be resolved upfront — saving time, minimizing re-
sources, and increase the net-value of circular collaborations.
The goal is to develop an overview of opportunities which can support structured and
transparent collaborations but also establishes a more shared process across the value
chain, enabling smoother transitions and faster alignment.
1.2
Research gaps and questions
From the Research Clarification described in chapter 3. There are found three research
gaps:
• Lack of common language about collaboration in CE transition.
• Lack of general approach which is not developed or focused on a specific industry
or case.
• Lack of focus on other than OEM or Brand owners as enabler of collaboration in CE
transition.
Based on the three identified research gaps this thesis will work with the following research
gap.
Research Gap: Lack of generic understanding for the implementation of collabora-
tion for CE transition
Based on the findings during the Research Clarifications research gaps, the first research
question (RQ1) was formulated. For a further understanding of the generic challenges
2
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:19==
leading to opportunities, the second research question (RQ2) was developed.
Research Question 1 aims to investigate the existing structure, and dynamics supported
in the literature for collaboration for CE transition in the technical cycle.
Research Question 2 aims to investigate the existing challenges in the literature for col-
laboration for CE transition in the technical cycle.
RQ 1: How is the literature supporting collaboration for CE transition?
• RQ 1.1: Which focus areas are found in the literature for supporting collabo-
ration for CE transition?
• RQ 1.2: Which dynamics are described in the literature supporting collabora-
tion for CE transition?
RQ 2: What are the existing challenges for collaboration for CE transition docu-
mented in the literature?
• RQ 2.1: In which focus areas are the challenges for collaboration for CE
transition?
• RQ 2.2: In which stages of the collaboration are the challenges for collabora-
tion for CE transition?

2
Chapter: Research Methodology
2.1
Design Research Methodology
This thesis uses the Design Research Methodology, type 1, design research, [1], illus-
trated in Figure 2.1. It aims to explore the state of the industry and literature, identify
gaps, and develop a further understanding for future development. The chosen research
design has a review-based Research Clarification followed by a comprehensive Descrip-
tive Study I. This thesis aims to better understand the existing situation and to identify the
factors most relevant to addressing the improvement of collaboration in the CE transition.
This thesis uses a qualitative approach to investigate the situation. Methods used are
interviews, observations, and written documents.
Figure 2.1: Illustration of DRM framework with the type 1 design research highlighted [1]
Research clarification
In this stage, an exploratory literature review is done to investigate the research area and
determine the aim, focus, and scope of the thesis.
A previous investigation of the field supports the exploratory literature review. The previ-
ous investigation was a preparation for this thesis and includes an ecosystem map of a
company whose current practices are an example of one of the gaps in the literature. By
identifying the extent of what remains to be solved and existing solutions of collaboration
for CE transition in both academia and current practice.
Descriptive study I
This thesis has a comprehensive DS-1 involving a literature review as well as an empirical
study to fill the lack of understanding on opportunities for collaboration in CE transition.
A comprehensive DS-I is used with the aim of gaining a more general understanding
of opportunities for collaboration in CE transition, which was identified as lacking in the
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
5
==PAGE:22==
Research Clarification. As a comprehensive study, this study includes: Systematic Liter-
ature Reviews, Semi-structured Interviews, and a Workshop, to understand the criteria of
success and opportunities for enabling collaboration for CE transition.
This process involves iterations where the understanding increases and gives rise to fur-
ther empirical studies. In each cycle of these steps, different methods are used, however,
this thesis is written as a linear process to minimize complexity for the reader.
The basis for this stage is the research questions developed from Section 3.5, because of
the relatively unexplored area of design. The research questions and the methodologies
used are illustrated on Figure 2.2 in Section 2.2 and further explained in ??.

Methodological framework
Figure 2.2: Methodological framework: Illustration of research questions, stages, and

2.2.1
Research methods and approach for Research Clarification
The research clarifications are developed through a preliminary study investigating col-
laboration in the literature and industry, exemplified by mapping the ecosystem of a value
chain company. Together with an exploratory literature review, the research questions,
gaps, and plan for the empirical study have been derived.
2.2.2
Research methods and approach for RQ1
Research Question 1 is addressed through a systematic literature review, ensuring that
the relevant academic literature is collected and analyzed in a structured and transparent
manner. The review process follows the methodology outlined by Biolchini et al. (2005)[2],
which includes the development of a review protocol, systematic search and selection,
data extraction, and synthesis of findings. The aim is to identify support and mechanisms
for collaboration for circular economy transition.
Followed by empirical data collected through an industry workshop, where participants
shared their needs and experience in solving them to understand expectations for support
that could facilitate their circular economy transition. The workshop gives a more detailed
understanding of the support needs for circular economy transition.
2.2.3
Research methods and approach for RQ2
Research Question 2 is addressed through a systematic literature review, ensuring that
the relevant academic literature is collected and analyzed in a structured and transparent
manner. The review process follows the methodology outlined by Biolchini et al. (2005)[2],
which includes the development of a review protocol, systematic search and selection,
data extraction, and synthesis of findings. The purpose of the review is to identify barriers
that hinder collaboration for the circular economy transition.
To complement and expand the findings from the literature, empirical data were collected
through three individual semi-structured interviews with industry representatives. These
interviews provided additional insights into practical barriers experienced by companies
and allowed for a more nuanced understanding of the challenges encountered in real-
world collaborative settings.
2.2.4
AI
AI has been used through the writing phase to ensure clear communication in the form of
grammar and sentence structure.

3
Research Clarification
3.1
Introductory Questions
To guide the Research Clarification phase, two areas of introductory questions are pro-
posed to understand the context and define the frame of the research and the main needs
in terms of collaboration in the Circular Economy transition.
The goal is to create a foundation of terminology, and strategies to understand where this
thesis can help narrow the gaps.
The first area is to develop an understanding and knowledge within the field of Circular
Economy in relation to linear economy and sustainability. This helps scope the landscape
that this thesis will be looking into collaboration for the Circular Economy transition.
The second area is an initial understanding of what role collaboration has in Circular Econ-
omy transition, and what terminology, and dynamics are described.
This chapter ends with the identification of research gaps and the development of research
questions.
Introductory Question 1:
How is CE conceptually described and how does it relate to sustainable develop-
ment?
Introductory Question 2:
How is collaboration described in relation to the Circular
Economy transitiont?

3.2
Method
3.2.1
Introductory Question 1:
To develop an understanding within the field of Circular Economy, an exploratory literature
review is done, with some additional gray literature.
To investigate and answer this question, the circular economy is searched as Title-ABS-
Key in the database Scopus, giving a result of 38.732 articles. Here the articles are
sorted after the amount of citations, assuming the higher citation, the higher validation,
relevance, and acceptance in the field. The following top 7 articles were chosen:
Exported 14 October 2024.
• [3] The Circular Economy – A new sustainability paradigm?, 4212 citations
• [4]Conceptualizing the circular economy: An analysis of 114 definitions, 4074 cita-
tions
• [5]A review on circular economy: The expected transition to a balanced interplay of
environmental and economic systems, 3605 citations
• [6]Circular Economy: The Concept and its Limitations, 2167 citations
• [7] Product design and business model strategies for a circular economy, 2102 ci-
tations
• [8] Towards circular economy implementation: A comprehensive review in context
of manufacturing industry, 1707 citations
• [9] Product services for a resource-efficient and circular economy - A review, 1324
citations
3.2.2
Introductory Question 2:
To answer this question, results from a preliminary study consisting of two systematic lit-
erature reviews, together with empirical data, were collected through a workshop with a
case company working with CE as its main business model.
The literature was collected through two search strings in Scopus, one with a focus on
sustainability and one on circular economy. All abstracts were scanned to filter out irrel-
evant publications.
The workshop aims to map the ecosystem of a value recovery company founded on the
principle of circular economy.
A more detailed description of the workshop and the ecosystem map can be found in
the appendix appendix B. While only the results will be described in this research clari-
fication. For this research clarification, a follow-up interview was conducted with one of
the participants from the workshop to collect additional thoughts, reflection, or experience.
As additional literature, ”The SDG Partnership Guidebook” [10] is used.
The book was written in collaboration with The Partnering Initiative (TPI) and The United
Nations Department of Economic and Social Affairs (UN DESA). TPI is an international
10
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:27==
NGO with a focus on the development of theory and practice of multi-stakeholder col-
laboration, guided by the 2030 Agenda and the 17 Sustainable Development Goals [11].
With its limited scope on multi-stakeholder collaboration, it is mainly used for collection of
terminology, and to understand and navigate in the landscape of terms and structure.
3.3
IQ 1: Overview of circular economy
3.3.1
Circular economy concept
3.3.2
Circular economy and sustainable development
Sustainability gained focus back in 1960, when evidence on global-scale environmental
risk was increasing, e.g., thin ozone, climate change, and biodiversity loss [3]. In 1987 The
Brundtland Commision provided the most commonly accepted definition of sustainability
as “development that meets the needs of the present without compromising the ability of
future generations to meet their own needs” ([3], p. 4.)
In this thesis the therm sustainable development is framed as; A concept describing the
aim of maintaining a balance of environmental, economic and social factors to meet the
needs of the present generation without compromising the future generations [3] [5] [6].
The linear economy is one of the main concerns to global sustainable development with
the flow of material and energy through the system. [6].
Circular economy (CE) is seen as an sustainable development strategy utilizing the ma-
terial and energy disposed in the linear economy in balance with economic and social
development. CE aims to close the open-ended extract-produce-use-dispose structure
from linear economy, by focusing on keeping the product use-value in circulation as long
as possible with the highest possible value and quality [6],[3]. The value of the resource
is highest, where the product keeps its intended function and thereby fully utilize the em-
bedded value [3].
Similar to sustainability is circular economy not solely focusing on economics in develop-
ment [3], but the three objective; the environmental, economic and social objective [6],
[3]. ”Successful circular economy contributes to all the three dimensions of sustainable
development” ([6], p. 3.). If a CE implementation only takes one or two of the three di-
mension of the sustainable development into account it may result in an implementation
that is not sustainable [4].
Booth CE and sustainable development share the global perspective and the relevance
of coordination and value co-creation between multiple actors [3]. According to [3] coop-
eration of different stakeholders is seen a necessity.
Time is also one of the main components of CE supporting the definition: ”To future gen-
eration” in sustainable development. The cycle process inherent in CE does also reflect
its focus on long-term sustainability, with resources and value managed through multiple
life cycles. Thereby making CE as a long-term solution [4].
Investigation done by [3] found that CE can be seen as ”a necessary”, ”one of multiple
necessary” and ”one option among others” for sustainable development depending on the
reference. Figure ?? shows a more detailed description of the different relationship types
mapped by [3], but the argument for the three main categories are:
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
11
==PAGE:28==
• The argument for CE as a necessary for sustainable development are by it being a
precondition for sustainable manufacturing. Considering a circular business mod-
els and supply chains as necessary condition for maintaining an economic growth
sustainable [3].
• The argument for CE as one of multiple necessary are adding that change of lifestyle
is also needed to sustain a long-term sustainable solution.
CE is therefore not
enough on its own [3].
• The argument for CE as the one option among others are that CE is a part of broader
sustainable strategies and an archetype of sustainable business models. Assuming
other business models and strategies also needs to be taken into account. Finding
the best possible to the context [3].
In this thesis Circular economy is seen as a solution for transformation of a system to a
sustainable one, if all commitment are made and the system implements a full transfor-
mation. This is under the assumption that a good design of a CE solution also gives in-
centive and facilitate lifestyle change. Because of its three objective, the long-term focus,
and global perspective, giving it a strong conditional relation to sustainable development.
The the transition an implementation of a CE system may require other solution to back-up
the system or may result in a trade in relation to sustainability, however the design should
be dynamic and circular economy should aim through iterations to be a main solution.
3.3.3
Circular economy frameworks and approaches
The approach to use circular economy as a concept have been illustrated through different
strategies and frameworks from multiple different angels. For further description, compar-
ison and discussion of circular economy for collaboration four frameworks are highlighted.
Each framework has a main focus how to address the value generated through circular
economy.
• The two material loop (biological or technical)
• The shape of the loop (slowing, narrowing, closing)
• The hierarchy of the loop
• The business strategy model CE scanner.
The biological and technical loop
The butterfly diagram focus on the purpose of the resource separating the cycles into bi-
ological and technical cycles, see figure 3.1 [12]. It contains a hierarchy of activities like
the 9R, where the most value is captured in the inner activity. The characterization of the
loops in material circles require a products or parts can be separated. A product consisting
of material from both the technical and biological cycle, needs to be separated to ensure
the materials can enter the right cycle. [7]. The biological and technical loop are also
focusing more on the activities which can be done to enable the cycle depending on the
context and function of the product e.g. ”Product for service” or ”Product of consumption”.
12
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:29==
Figure 3.1: The butterfly diagram with biological and technical cycle [12]
The shape of the loop: Slowing, closing, narrowing
Slowing, closing and narrowing is terminology introduced to describe the resource loops
and categorize design and business model strategies. Authors describe the flow of the
resources through the system[7]:
• Slowing, focus on slowing down the material flow by extending the utilization period.
This could be through design for longevity, introduction of service loops, repair, abil-
ity for upgrade, intensify the use.
• Closing, focus on closing the loop by recirculating material back into the loop. This
could e.g. be reuse, refurbish, remanufacturing, re-purpose, recycling or cascade if
looking into the technical cycle.
• Narrowing, focus on resource efficiency by using less resources for each product.
E.g. reducing resources in the production-process is also narrowing the loop.
Together, these strategies emphasize that a circular economy is not only about closing
the loop but also about slowing and narrowing resource flows, ensuring that materials and
products are used more efficiently and for longer periods [7].
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
13
==PAGE:30==
Figure 3.2: Narrowing, slowing and closing the loop illustrated graphically [7]
The hierarchy
Describe how the different circular strategies has an hierarchical order, reflecting the pre-
ferred sequence of actions to maximize value and resource efficiency.
At the top of the hierarchy are strategies such as refuse, rethink, and reduce, which aim to
minimize resource use altogether by designing smarter products, reducing material inputs,
or reconsidering consumption patterns. These strategies are the most resource-efficient,
as they prevent waste from occurring in the first place [6]..
When products already exist, the next preferred strategies involve extending their lifes-
pan through reuse, repair, refurbishment, remanufacturing, or repurposing [6]. These
approaches preserve much of the embedded value — including labor, energy, and ma-
terials — already invested in creating the product. By keeping products and components
in productive use, companies avoid the need for new raw materials and delay the point at
which materials enter waste streams [6]..
At the bottom of the hierarchy are strategies such as recycling and energy recovery. While
recycling allows for material recovery, it destroys the added production value that was built
into the product during manufacturing — such as the design, processing, and assembly
steps [6].
The 9R framework can bee seen on figure 3.3[4]. Showing the increasing preservation or
added value by applying the different development strategies from the bottom to top.
14
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:31==
Figure 3.3: 9R Hierarchy [4]
The CE scanner
Last and not least, there are the circular economy scanner designed to support manu-
facturing companies identify the multiple strategies which can enable circular economy
principle [13]. The tool includes 32 circular strategies all categorized according to specific
business processes, providing insights into how these strategies can be integrated into an
existing company. The possible business model strategies support the circular economy
principle on the the technical circle. [13]. With the 32 circular strategies the tool has the
ability to be a configuration of all possible circular strategies helping navigation for man-
ufacturing companies. It includes the hierarchical structure to of the different strategies
helping business understand the trade-offs and synergies among the different strategies.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
15
==PAGE:32==
Figure 3.4: CE scanner from [13]
The business models developed for According to Bocken [7], is a Circular Business mod-
els seen as main driver for innovation in CE by the argument that the Business model
defines the architecture of the business and scalability [7]. It is important to ensure a
good business model because when implemented it can be difficult to change [7].
Because of the size of the change in introducing a CE product, the ”go to market” or ”cap-
turing value” strategies requires development of a business model along side a design
strategies, see figure ?? .
System thinking describes the overlook which is important when developing an new busi-
ness model and design strategies. It should help avoid rebound effects, and wider con-
sequences of adopting the business model on the environmental, economic and social
perspective. [7]. A rebound effect is when the implemented CE strategy does not give
the expected environmental effect, because the consumption practices does not follow
the expected pattern [6]. This could be the saved resources and money generated from
the strategy being used on other consumption practices. Customers could as an exam-
ple spend their cost savings on other ”polluting activities” [7]. An example could be the
car-sharing service, where the consumer were expected to change culture of owning a
car, thereby having fewer cars on the street. A rebound effect for this could be a service
creating a second car for the consumer or for consumers not owning a car it is the easier
way of transport instead of public transportation or bike. Thereby not resulting in the ex-
pected environmental gain.
The dynamic interaction between business models, product design, supply chain and cus-
tomer is essential [8].Production and distribution is no longer enough, the consumption
phase has to be integrated, what may also be one of the main challenges [4].
16
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:33==
Value chain layers With a value chain perspective the circular economy strategies and
the implementation of a closed loop have created new players to the value chain. These
player are supporting the new life cycle of products. It could be companies whose value
proposition is to help slowing the material loop by offering repair, maintenance, upgrade
of the products as ”Maintenance and Repair services”.Or it could be companies closing
the loop by offering remanufacturing, refurbishment, repurpose of the product or recycling
of the material like ”Value recovery”. These are examples of additional layers to the value
chain in the technical cycle, which has been identified by Ready2Loop [14] as illustrated
in fig. 3.5. The areas is under development why other layers could arise however this
thesis are using the defined value chain layers from Ready2Loop [14] when analyzing the
industry.
Figure 3.5: Illustration of value chain based on Ready2Loop.org
3.3.4
IQ 1: Conclusion
Circular economy(CE) is a concept aiming to decouple economic value from material re-
sources, unlike the linear economy, with its the extract-produce-use-dispose depending
on the extraction of new materials [4].
Circular economy is a concept described with various different approaches and focus.
Some papers describes circular economy in relation to sustainable development [3], or
the planetary boundaries [6] with a high focus on the environmental gains. Others focus
more on the economy, possible circular business models(CBM) and their implementation
in the industry [8].[9], [7],[5]. Lastly ones focus on unifying the conceptualizing of circular
economy in the academia and industry, [4].
In this thesis Circular Economy os defined as a concept replacing the open-ended linear
concept by introducing a closed loop of material and energy. This is done with focusing on
keeping the embedded value of the products and energy in circulation as long as possible
with the highest possible value and quality. Thereby introducing a hierarchy of possible
circular solutions [6],[3]. This can be achieved by implementing circular strategies in the
production, distribution, and consumption processes [4]. It operates on the micro, meso,
and macro levels, accomplishing the three bottom lines, environmental, economic, and
social [4]. There will in this thesis only be looked on circular economy from the technical
side of the butterfly diagram [12] and the value chain layers will be following the proposed
from [14] when identifying and structuring companies.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
17
==PAGE:34==
3.4
IQ 2: Collaboration for circular economy transition
3.4.1
Circular economy transition
Collaboration The literature describes collaboration as an enabler for transition to cir-
cular economy. This could be inter-organizational collaboration between a networks of
companies on material and energy utilization or to prolong life cycle of products [6]. It
could be industrial symbiosis(IS) by exchanging byproducts, taking waste output from one
process and use it as feedstock for another process [7] or sharing services like cleaning/-
maintenance [5]. Eco-industrial parks (EIP) is an example of industrial symbiosis used
in a collaborative network of geographic proximity [8]. A Case example on this could be
Kalundborg Eco-Industrial Park, in Denmark. Kalundborg is a bottom-up created park.
Starting with industrial symbiosis created by companies discovering the opportunity of
using each others by-products [5]. Five co-located companies have economic profit from
by-product sharing. In the beginning the reduction of environmental effects was not a
motivation factor and only a later realization. The same has been found in similar cases
where they were unaware of environmental benefit. [5].
Different types of collaboration was found described in the literature and are placed in
table 3.1. These collaboration types are not necessity between two actors but could also
be between a network of actors [6].
There is a tendency for the papers/articles to describe collaboration for implementation of
CE principle by performing a literature study followed by a focus on a specific company
([21], [22]), a case in a company ([15]), or the industry ([23], [16], [17], [20]). Lacking a
more general approach like with the collaboration guide done by the UN [10]. The UN,
and other development actors are trying to build a more systematic engagement with
businesses towards the SDG’s [11], seeing businesses as key development actors. The
SDG Partnership Guidebook [10], introduces the framework ”Partnership Life cycle”, the
key ”Building Blocks” of successful partnerships, and the ”Journey To Action” plan which
will is shortly described in ??. These frameworks focus on enabling and structuring multi-
stakeholder collaboration towards the 17 sustainability goals (SDG) [11]. The motivation
is the partners using the same tool should be able to compare notes, and placement in
the process and identify needs of support. Following the same path should also makes it
possible to quickly identify deal breakers or specific issues that need to be solved [10].
Even though the focus of the industry papers were specific, the same challenges and
barriers seem to show up, especially in the challenge of transformation of the ecosystem
have to happen collectively in the industry [21], [16], [15].
e.g. in the EV battery sector a shift to CBM has implications on the entire electrical and bat-
tery value chain [20]. ”... the importance of multi-actor collaboration and network-centric
CBM innovation within the EV battery ecosystem highlighting the need for an ecosystem-
level perspective in circular economy research” [20], p.16. The government together with
the brand owners are seen as key responsible for steering the innovation ecosystem to
a more sustainable solution [21]. Simultaneously the government is also seen as the en-
couraging factor where the roles of responsibility in the value chain and the sustainable
goal are not clearly defined [21], [22].
This thesis will tackle collaboration as a partnership with the intention of providing re-
sources to each other for transitioning to CE with the aim of co-value creation. The value

creation has to be higher than the resources of partnering, transitioning, and developing
requiring a structured process with alignment and information sharing.
The partners has to put effort form outside of the normal value chain before it is collabo-
ration, this can be through the resources of knowledge, time, workforce, skills, material,
technology, energy or finance. This resource is added to solve a problem through develop
innovation in infrastructure, technology or business strategy.
Net-value creation
Net-value creation of the collaboration should be aimed higher than if the companies work
alone. Net-value creation covers the economic return of investment of resources. Often
in order to have motivation from companies and investors their need to be a economic
return of investment [5]. In the Eco-industrial parks the aim of the collaboration is to have
a competitive advantage as a collective [8]. For example could the net-value creation in
industrial symbiosis be lowering overall operation cost. This could be from substituting
virgin energy and material or reduce cost from discharge fees or disposal of by-product
[7], [5]. The driver of industrial symbiosis is often economic benefits, however it is also
providing an environmental benefit as well as a in-direct benefits which could be better rep-
utation, ability to attract and retain employees, increase of supply security and innovation
[5]. Some researcher also question how to determine the net-sustainability contribution,
because it may from a network perspective perform better than for the individual [6] as an
example Korhonen argument that it in some cases it could be justified for the companies
to maximize the by-product flow instead of reducing it, because the other actors in the
network may use it as alternative to raw/virgin-material and source for energy.
The improvement or positive outcome should be compared to situation before. The com-
plexity in quantifying the before and after may result in systems where the improvement
is more a problems displacement or problems shifting from one part of the system to an-
other [6]. The flow of materials and energy crossing organizations, administration and
geographical boundaries is complex, changing and dynamic [6]. It has been found that
the design structure of the system has to be developed together with the business model.
Therefor if the business model is kept static it may affect the innovation of the design
structure and limit the net-value creation. The net value created by the collaboration has
to be understand by every partner to sustain the partnership. Otherwise a partner will
want to seek other opportunities seeing the value chain situation as unsustainable [10].
Alignment of the strategic vision and ensuring net-value creation s therefore seen two
highlighted dynamics in the literature.
Orchestrator
There are questions in areas of who has the responsibility, risk and control or what is the
network decision-making platform and who organize the collaboration [6]. This introduce
the role of an orchestrator. They are mainly mentioned in the research as experts or ac-
tors, enabling the management of collaboration.
Not only innovative concepts are a necessity also innovative actors is requires for a suc-
cessful transition to CE [5]. It may be essential that the companies have resources in the
shape of these actors. The actors works as an enabler with their capability to create link
and suitable patterns for sustainability and circular business models [5]. Alignment and
assessment of companies capabilities and readiness are therefore mentioned as some of
the initial steps in collaboration [16], [15]. Circular economy requires a higher involvement
of the collaborating partners for a successful sustainable development [5]. The role of en-
abling that involvement by navigating the partners is suggested undertaken by the orches-
trator described through different synonyms in the industry like cross-functional leadership
20
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:37==
[23], platform owner [16], the matchmaker and promoters [15], driver of the supply chain
[17]. The orchestrator are in the industry described as a critical role in the implementation
of the strategy [16] and is enabling the lack of coordination [23] in the ecosystem. Many
of the articles agree on the need for an orchestrator, however they all focus on different
roles. Though do they agree that the role carrier is one with high influence on the product
and resources like the large manufacturing companies and brand owners [16],[15], [17].
The value recovery company, enables the link between several value chains and toke a
role as orchestrator in innovative collaborations. This were shown in the capabilities like
coordination, managing a portfolio of partners, match-making capabilities, and sharing
expertise in recycling. This is unlike the orchestrator description made by the articles of
the industry. The articles focus mainly on the brand owners or manufacturing compa-
nies whom has financial and product ownership. Another value recovery company Stena
Recycling has also developed a managing consultant arm, undertaking task of helping
transitioning companies to circular economy, acting as a independent mediator orches-
trating dialog and cooperation among the value chain partners [24]. There case examples
are describes through a collaborative approach setting them self as the orchestrator [19].
Value recovery companies taking the role as orchestrators can be an argumentation on
expanding the frameworks in the literature to not solely look onto manufacture or OEM.
Thereby not depending on the OEM or brand owners to initiate or specialize in the trans-
formation. Only one paper are suggesting the Raw material provider could take the role
as facilitator of value chain transformation, however this were more with the focus on there
ability to innovate new materials enabling the rest of the value chain to transition [17].
Competition
Path-dependency describes the phenomenon where the first innovation in the market
achieves the best markets and receives most awareness thereby becomes stronger than
innovations joining the market later on [6]. This is also said to be the ”survival of the first”
not the fittest. [6]. There will be competition between existing and new CE models as
well as the linear alternatives. The existing are mainly focusing on conventional recy-
cling and low quality of material utilization. The new innovative CE models focusing more
on product reuse, remanufacturing, refurbishment and sharing have to compete against
the established structure and culture of recycling [4]. Path dependency may therefore
work against new innovative CE models from penetrating the market. [6]. Business also
tends continue the old way of doing instead of adopt new technologies or models. As a
result thereof technological superiority and management does not guarantee long-term
success.
An opportunity could be that the first business that won in the path dependency could be
introduced to collaboration with the emerging new innovative CE models. Thereby using
the strength they have on the market as a resource.
CE can increase a company’s brand value and corporate reputation, this could be through
sustainability performance, academic validation of sustainability, or high-ranking collabo-
ration with leading companies, [25], [26].
For the pharmaceutical industry [23], the competition of the market limits the transforma-
tion; four large companies highlight the main barriers to the transition towards a circular
supply chain management (CSCM) were information sharing, cooperation, and collabora-
tion together with a lack of coordination in the supply chain network [23]. The competition
in the market hinders the exchange of expertise and information [23].

3.4.2
IQ 2: Conclusion
Collaboration are seen as an enabler to implementation of circular strategies for all actors
of the value chain. There are different types of collaboration which all are affecting the
value chain (value chain, Sector, cross-sector, multi stakeholder, innovative and collabo-
ration for regulation). The system, dynamic and development of collaboration are complex
and therefore requiring an orchestrator.
The orchestrator were in the academia mostly a role described of manufacturing firms and
required many resources. With the ecosystem map in a value recovery company is was
found that this company also could deliver the orchestrator role. One of the main barriers
are competition and the amount of time and resources used in initial stages of developing
collaboration, affecting the net-value creation of the partnership.
There are in the literature lacking more general research and understanding of collabo-
ration for circular economy transition in the value chain. The focus are highly on man-
ufacturing companies and OEMs. This creates a gap in support for implementation of
CE principles — for all partners to enable collaboration and distribute roles. Such an ap-
proach would reduce dependence on the manufacturing companies or product owner to
initiate or specialize in the transformation. Most of the papers uses specific value chains
for the case company of industry investigated with out implementing it to a more general
take on a more general value chain.
A list of different collaboration types have be developed showing the many different types
of collaboration and intent there was found. The focus on net-value creation and align-
ment of partners knowledge of circular economy are found as two important fundamental
elements in collaboration for circular economy transition.
Conceptual confusion
Comes from the different understanding of what the net-value creation and sustainable
development is. If not aware of the different understandings or goals it can be a barrier
in both the process of innovate the business model and design strategy. It may lead to
misleading results in knowledge accumulation attempts. It can also be the barrier for
advancing [4].
This underlines the need of an academic definition of terminologies for alignment of the
target. Using value chain from ready 2 and the technical cycle. Add additional focus on
how there is a lack of detailed support more appicable for collaboration with element more
than just a case description. To help guide the collaboration.

3.5
Research gaps and questions
From the Research Clarification described in chapter 3. There are found three research
gaps:
• Lack of common language about collaboration in CE transition.
• Lack of general approach which is not developed or focused on a specific industry
or case.
• Lack of focus on other than OEM or Brand owners as enabler of collaboration in CE
transition.
Based on the three identified research gaps this thesis will work with the following research
gap.
Research Gap: Lack of generic understanding for the implementation of collabora-
tion for CE transition
Based on the findings during the Research Clarifications research gaps, the first research
question (RQ1) was formulated. For a further understanding of the generic challenges
leading to opportunities, the second research question (RQ2) was developed.
Research Question 1 aims to investigate the existing structure, and dynamics supported
in the literature for collaboration for CE transition in the technical cycle.
Research Question 2 aims to investigate the existing challenges in the literature for col-
laboration for CE transition in the technical cycle.
RQ 1: How is the literature supporting collaboration for CE transition?
• RQ 1.1: Which focus areas are found in the literature for supporting collabo-
ration for CE transition?
• RQ 1.2: Which dynamics are described in the literature supporting collabora-
tion for CE transition?
RQ 2: What are the existing challenges for collaboration for CE transition docu-
mented in the literature?
• RQ 2.1: In which focus areas are the challenges for collaboration for CE
transition?
• RQ 2.2: In which stages of the collaboration are the challenges for collabora-
tion for CE transition?

4.1.1
Planning
Development of search string
The focus of the systematic literature review is to identify how the literature is supporting
collaboration for CE transition in the technical cycle.
The keywords have been developed with the focus of five criteria:
1. The papers have to have a main focus on the circular economy or circular economy
transition.
2. The collaboration in the papers has to be with a focus on the SC, VC, or in the
ecosystem.
3. The paper has to contain an industry focus in the technical cycle.
4. The paper has to contain the dynamic or role of orchestrators or organization.
5. The paper has to contain collaboration or partners.
The following keywords are chosen after testing:
CE:
”circular”
Placement organization or orchestration of collaboration:
”supply chain”
”value chain”
”ecosystem”
Technical cycle and industry:
”manufacture”
”producer”
”industry”
Dynamic of organization or orchestrator:
”orchestrator”
”organization”
Collaboration or collaborator:
”collaborate”
”partner”
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition

==PAGE:42==
The search string is adjusted based on whether it needs to be broadened or made more
specific, depending on the relevance of the papers.
The term ”orchestrator” was added after identifying the value recovery company as one
within the ecosystem workshop conducted during the initial study and research clarifi-
cation. This was done to gain a deeper understanding of this dynamic and how it is
supported.
When testing a search using only the combination of:
TITLE(”Supply chain” OR ”Value chain” OR ”Ecosystem”) AND TITLE-ABS-KEY(”Manufacture”
OR ”Producer” OR ”Industry”) AND TITLE-ABS-KEY(”Orchestrator”) AND KEY(”Circular”)
Only 3 documents were found, where one was focusing on the biological cycle of the food
sector see fig. 4.1. By adding the ”organization” the search gave 99 results.
Adding the ”collaborate” and ”partner” and limiting it to articles or literature review the
search ended up giving 16 papers.

4.1.2
Execution
The search string was run in Scopus on the 16/10/2024.
Selection of papers
The procedure for selection of relevant studies is illustrated in fig. 4.2. The publication are
read and sorted using the following list from top to bottom:
• Filter 1: Read title, keywords
• Filter 2: Read abstract
• Filter 3: Look through the paper if there are developed a support
• Filter 4: Read the complete article
If a article meets exclusion criteria EC-1 or EC-2, it is removed. Filter 1 eliminated two
articles based on their titles, as they focused on the biological cycle. Filter 2 didn’t remove
any article however, the majority were excluded in Filter 3, either because their support
primarily focused on technology implementation or because they lacked a support.

Extraction
At this stage, the goal is to collect support elements from the articles. A single article
may contain multiple support elements. After each paper has been reviewed, its support
elements are extracted and assigned a unique ID number.
When reviewing the article the following criteria are collected:

Supporting element characterization
The aim is to compare the supporting elements by creating an overview of their strength.

overview of the strength of the chosen 6 support elements, they are organized into a table,
each placed in its own column fig. 4.6. The rows represent the building block categories
from [10]. This framework was chosen because it was developed as the fundamental
boxes for creating multi-stakeholder collaboration for sustainable development.

and Management & Leadership. If a support element aligns with any of these categories,

Element 1: Detailed archetypes of CBM
The EV study by [20] focuses on identifying potential archetypes of circular business mod-
els (CBM) for EV batteries fig. 4.7. It investigates the specific co-creation value and the
implications for company collaborations when extending, sharing, or looping EV batteries.
Figure 4.7: Element 1: Detailed archetypes of CBM [20] ??
Net-value creation or the value co-creation is a key element highlighted in [20], depend-
ing on the intent and commitment to collaboration, as well as the ownership dynamics
of the product.The key business model elements highlighted in the support are: Value
proposition, co-creation, delivery and capture.
The aim is to provide a clear description of Key Stakeholder collaboration as guidance
for stakeholders among the value chain [20]. However, the main focus is on OEMs (or-
chestrators), with energy utility companies and material suppliers as project partners. By
utilizing CBM archetypes, OEMs can assess, develop, and scope new business models
[20], ensuring alignment with Governance, Management, and Operational structures.
The ownership structure and how they can change influence the design, the adoption and
implementation can be seen as the the Accountability and Commitment of the actors. The
suggested CBM are characterized on the different levels of product-service combination
in the value co-creation.
The potential CBM archetypes involves the Mutual Benefits of stakeholder partnerships
within the ecosystem, highlighting the importance of co-creating value and fostering in-
novation. Since each stakeholder brings unique competencies, alignment through col-
laborative mechanisms is suggested, such as strategic alliances, to secure access to
resources, expertise, and markets.
Element 2: Two stage ecosystem
In the study of 6 manufacturing companies a more generic two-stage process for trans-
formation framework was developed, fig. 4.8.
Figure 4.8: Element 2: Two stage ecosystem [16] ??
In the framework are mainly focusing on the Orchestrator Function. The requirements for
the orchestrator role is to be a focal firm who need to be resourceful, stable, simplifying the
34
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:51==
connection between different stakeholders, encouraging innovation and niche creation,
and can enforce game rules, promote transparency and imposing sanctions [16]. The
others actors in the value chain are seen as ecosystem partners.
The framework consist of two-stages.
The first stage is the assessment of the readiness level of the ecosystem for transforma-
tion involving. This stage involves assessments of business model (Net-value creation,
Mutual benefits), ecosystem partners (Organizational values, Accountability and commit-
ment) and external environment. The assessment stage is to help identify and understand
gaps. For managers in the companies it is to identify new opportunities, gather external
information and understand their own capacity in relation to CE transformation [16].
The second stage is orchestration mechanisms for transformation covering Trust and
Transparency, Leadership, Communication, Learning & Knowledge sharing and Gover-
nance, Management & Operational structure table 4.4. The orchestration mechanisms
are standardization, nurturing, and negotiation to achieve benefits of circular implemen-
tation, [16]. Identifying actions to manage and influence the transition or transformation
of the core, peripheral and new ecosystem partners [16].
Table 4.4: Orchestration mechanisms stage 2 [16]
Standardization
Action to define and legitimize the goal for ecosystem partners.
Promoting establishment of industrial standards.
Pursuing technological standard co-development.
Seeking formal certification through broader adoption.
Nurturing
Action to ensure the necessary resources and capabilities
Bearing early investment to reduce uncertainty.
Support ecosystem competences development.
Sharing core knowledge and intellectual property within industry.
Negotiation
Action to coordinate the ecosystem participants.
Establish give-and-take rules for ecosystem.
Reducing likelihood of conflicts through relational interdependence.
Controlling inclusion of new partners based on risk and benefit analysis.
Element 3: Governance mechanism
In the study of the plastic (PUR) industry a framework of commitment is investigated with
focus on vertical and horizontal collaborations [17], fig. 4.9.
Figure 4.9: Element 3: Governance mechanism [17] ??
Collaborations commitment: Accountability and commitment, and the stakeholders hav-
ing the Orchestrator function as enabler and facilitator are structured into four models.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
35
==PAGE:52==
Shultz, [17] highlight two facilitators The Raw-Material Producers and End-Application
Producers argumentation that this is because of their position at each end of the supply
chain.
This include the description of different dilemma structures: Power structure, Mutual ben-
efits. The dilemma structures are grouped into one-sided or many-sided. One-sided
means asymmetric situation where one actor can exploit another. Many-sided means a
symmetric situation where all actors can equally exploit each others [17]. Through the four
models Key stakeholders and their enabling or facilitating actions are described together
with their interactions of Governance, management, operations structures.
Table 4.5: Vertical and horizontal collaboration by [17]
Vertical
collaboration: Individual self commitmen
Overcome one-sided dilemma. This could be a firm making a commitment to
a stakeholder on not exploiting them e.g. Warranty, repair service [17]. Asym-
metric power because the End-Application Producer require it from suppliers by
”Taking responsibility”.[17]
Vertical
collaboration: Service for Individual Commitment
Overcome partners one-sided dilemma through a service e.g. certification, as-
sessment, consultant [17]. End-Application producer delivers commitment ser-
vice to suppliers who is on highly competitive market and can’t take CE initiatives
alone. By provide support to supplier with resources and training [17]
Horizontal collaboration: Collective self commitment
Competing firms collaborate in certain areas to overcome a many-sided dilemma
however still compete in other areas. “co-opetition” allow the firms to combine
their strength and resources to achieve mutual goal. It has to be a win-win situ-
ation where both firms gain for the effort [17].
PULL -
From End-Application producer
Large or well-organized multinational brand facing pressure from end-consumer
to move towards CE and can receive moral gratification by offering CE-products.
Could be industry e.g. mattress, furniture, automotive, building or construction
[17].
PUSH -
From Raw Material Producers
Can create closed-loop for a industry, innovate and create inter-industrial or inter-
sectoral open-loops, incentivize up-stream and down-stream [17].

This conceptual model identifies four strategic approaches to enable business model scal-
ability depending on the commitment and intent of collaboration. These are categorized
along two dimensions: closed/open business model and efficiency/adaptability centered
fig. 4.10 [27].

The model focus also highlight the dynamic capabilities necessary for the organizations
to transition between the different strategic approaches [27]. The focus on absorbing
external ideas and opportunities together with the Accountability and commitment of col-
laboration with Key stakeholders, depending on chosen business model. The model give
an overview of what is needed by the company and what Net-value creation the business
model strategy should provide.
Table 4.6: Strategic approaches, [27]
Closed and Efficiency-centered
Internal organization to improve scalability and reduce fixed costs.
Commitment focus on utilizing internal resources and capabilities.
Efficiency through standardization, automation and centralization.
Closed and Adaptability-centered
Utilize internal resource while being open to external influence.
Commitment focus on absorbing external ideas and opportunities
Adaptability through listening, experimenting and adapting strategies to scale up.
Open and Efficiency-centered
Looking outside the organization for collaboration while aiming for efficiency.
Commitment focus by engagement in partnerships that allow shared labor and
resources.
Efficiency through achieving economies of scale through collaborative strategies.
Open and Adaptability-centered
Collaboration with external partners to innovate and create new opportunities.
Commitment focus on collaboration with customers and stakeholders in the de-
velopment process.
Adaptability through integrating external insights into the business model.

Element 5: Promoter community
A case company (W&M) developing a Cradle-2-Cradle (C2C) product strategy co-developed
a network of partners to support the design strategy this are structured through a team of
promotors who orchestrated the network of partners and suppliers fig. 4.11,[15].
Figure 4.11: Element 5: Promoter community [15] ??
The promotors roles, are the role of matchmaking, with relevant partners and orchestrate
the overall network. why Communication are placed in between all promoters.
Here a promoter is defined as a “individuals who actively and intensively support the
innovation process” [15].
The promoters are structured into five types table 4.7. These types describe the role and
responsibility distribution in between the promoters; the Orchestrator function, their Power
structure, Accountability and commitment. The promoter type power and universal speaks
into the Senior-level commitment and Leadership. Where the Alignment of interest along
the value chain are shown in the relationship and power promoter. The process promoter
has a responsibility of Organizational values.
The structure and interaction of the promoters in the firm level, value chain level and
linking level illustrate how the Governance, management, operational structures could be
structured.
Table 4.7: Promoter types
The Power
Pushes innovation through hierarchical power.
The Expert
Contribute with expert knowledge.
The Relationship
Facilitate and build up innovation-related relationships inside and out-
side the company.
The Process
Have knowledge of the processes in the organization and translate the
different languages spoken in the firm.
The Universal
A combination of expert and power and either relationship or process.

4.2.1
Analysis and discussion
Bibliometric
When comparing the publishing years of the papers, most are from 2021. However, there
has been an increasing number of publications in the field from 2022 to 2024 fig. 4.13a.
The included papers are from 2019, 2021, and 2024, with three of them published in 2021.
Regarding the exclusion criteria fig. 4.13b, a significant number of papers were excluded
due to their focus on Industry 4.0 (EC-2). In 2024 alone, three papers were excluded
for this reason. This could suggest that collaboration and partnership are increasingly
discussed in the literature, along with implementing Industry 4.0, which is becoming an
area of increasing focus.
The number of citations tends to be lower for more recent papers fig. 4.13c, which is
expected since it takes time for citations to accumulate. The included papers are among
those with a higher number of citations.
Among the Journals, only two had more than one paper included in the search: ”The Jour-
nal of Cleaner Production” with three papers, followed by ”The Journal of Environmental
Management” fig. 4.13d. All the papers included come from different journals.
40
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:57==
(a) Publish year of papers
(b) Exclusion criteria and publish year
(c) Average citation
(d) Journal of papers
Figure 4.13: Excluded and included papers
Paper comparison
While all included articles explore approaches to circular economy transition and the im-
plementation of new circular strategies or business models, they differ in structure. Some
focus on specific strategies and case studies [15], while others provide an overview of
potential business models [27], [20]. Only two articles take a broader approach, empha-
sizing implementation and partner dynamics [16], [17].
The extracted elements vary in detail. Some serve as guides for transition [16], while
others identify possible solutions [20], [27]. Others primarily describe case studies to il-
lustrate the application of collaboration mechanisms and dynamics [17], [15].
Focus areas found in the literature for collaboration for CE transition.
• Primary focus on product manufacturers and product owners:
While empirical data is collected from multiple supply chain actors, most studies
primarily seek solutions for product manufacturers or product owners, often identi-
fying them as orchestrators [16], [15], [20]. Only [27] emphasizes strategic partner-
ships, while [17] highlights two key facilitators: Raw-Material Producers and End-
Application Producers(product manufacturer). The Raw-Material producer is in [17]
described in what would be a value recovery company on the value chain from the
research clarification fig. 3.5.
• Types of Collaboration:
Two articles focus on value chain collaboration [16], [15] one on collaboration for
regulation [20] and then there are two focusing on industry or cross-sector collabo-
ration [27],[17]. Two papers [17] and [16].
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
41
==PAGE:58==
• The dynamic change depending on the type of collaboration:
Two papers [17], [15] include the change of dynamics depending on whether the
collaboration is between the value chain or industry (competing companies). [15]
adds the additional need for ”Linking Partners” (governmental, political, industry as-
sociations, etc.) for the competing collaboration.
• Collaboration initiative and level of commitment:
Collaboration commitment [17] and intensity [20], [27] are described as factors in-
fluencing:
The choice of circular business model [20].
The scalability [27]
The environmental impact of CE initiatives [17].

Commitment and accountability:
All papers incorporate some level or intent of commitment and accountability within their
structure. This suggests that aligning commitment in collaborations and partnerships can
have a high impact and that understanding its requirements is essential for participating
actors.
Governance, Management and Operational Structure:
All papers, except one [27], include aspects of operational structure and management
in their support. This could highlight the importance of structuring collaborations, possi-
bly reflecting or indicating the challenges and complexities of managing and structuring
circular economy transitions.
Categories with strong support:
The following categories received support in four out of the six support elements. Indicat-
ing they have a strong role in circular economy collaboration: ”Net-Value Creation,” ”Key
Stakeholders,” and ”Orchestrator Function.”
They are also all placed in the fundamental group.
Management and leadership and Structure and Set-up:
In the block of management and leadership and the block of structure and set-up, there
is a lower amount of categories supported by the elements. There isn’t enough data to
draw firm conclusions, but this could suggest:
42
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:59==
• Collaborations may still be struggling with fundamental and relationship-building as-
pects, requiring stronger foundations before addressing management and structure.
• The building block structure used in this analysis may not fully align with circular
economy transitions, potentially missing relevant criteria.
4.2.2
Scoping SLR
This data-collection process has been documented and structured using the systematic
literature review process described in [2]. Evaluation of the planned protocol followed in
the execution phase showed some boundaries.
The systematic literature review identified five articles that included support focused on
collaboration for circular economy transition. Excluded articles mainly focused on tech-
nological implementation rather than collaboration. The chosen exclusion and inclusion
factors excluded articles were mainly with a higher focus on technological implementa-
tion than collaboration. There was a limitation in the low number of included articles and
the high number of excluded articles with a focus on technological implementations. The
included articles had a main focus on product manufacturers, which is also a boundary
for the aim of a more general analysis.
The included studies featured papers with a relatively high amount of empirical data gath-
ered through interviews and workshops across different parts of the value chain. Addi-
tionally, some papers provided a high level of detail but focused on a single case example.
No gray literature or snowballing methods were used to extract additional papers. The
primary focus of this SLR is to understand the collaboration elements and dynamics de-
scribed in the literature from the search string.
4.3
Phase 3: Support elements for collaboration for CE transition
In the support elements developed for circular economy (CE) transition through collabo-
ration, the main focus is on the platform owner or facilitator. The papers had an increased
focus on manufacturing companies as the orchestrators or enablers of ecosystem transi-
tion [17], [16], [15]. These studies also identify the orchestrators role and responsibility of
supporting the rest of the value chain [15], supply chain [17], or ecosystem partners [16],
acting as investors providing resources to enable the transition.
In the research clarification, it was identified that a value recovery company can also act
as an orchestrator, using its knowledge and expertise as key resources.
To gain a more diverse understanding of the orchestrator role, a workshop is developed
to explore the needs of a value recovery company. The results from this workshop will
then be used to further analyze and extract support elements from the comparison matrix.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
43
==PAGE:60==
4.3.1
Workshop
The aim of the workshop is to understand the needs, challenge and opportunities for the
value recovery company with their work on collaboration for circular economy transition.
Participants
This company works mainly with collaboration in the value chain like the focus of [16] and
[15]. These were determined by the experience in the company and knowledge on ex-
isting collaboration. The participants of this workshop were responsible for supply chain,
partnership and business innovation.
There were 3 participants to the workshop.
Scope
The workshop was scoped to focus on the value recovery company’s challenges and
experience, with the aim of identifying and understanding their needs when collaborating
with other actors for circular economy transition.
Structure and execution
The workshop was made in Miro for all participants to enter. Five questions were devel-
oped to collect experience and challenges in development of collaboration. The structure
of the workshop was the following.
1. The participants gets one question.
2. The participants has 5 minutes to brainstorm answers and write notes in Miro.
3. The participants has 1 minute to place 1 vote on the challenge they find most im-
portant to solve.
The five question were the following:
1. What challenges do you experience?
2. What benefits would they bring if they were solved?
3. What challenges have you already tried to solve/answer?
4. Did they improve?
5. Do you have en idea of what factors impacts the problem or why the solution did not
work?
After all the questions the participants would have placed in total 5 votes each. To help
structuring the answers the four blocks from the building blocks developed by [10] were
used like in the comparison matrix of the support elements in fig. 4.6. This allows for
easier comparison later with the frameworks. The Miro board un-used is illustrated in
fig. 4.15.
44
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:61==
Figure 4.15: Workshop board in Miro
Results from workshop
The workshop identified the main challenges (gray boxes), along with smaller challenges
(yellow), wishes (green), and values (orange), representing the benefits of solving these
challenges. Additionally, solutions (purple) were noted for challenges the company has
already addressed, and questions (blue) were raised about roles and dynamics.
Top five challenges identified in the workshop table 4.10: The following five challenges
received the highest votes and had the most value if solved.
1. Structure and Set-up: Resource allocation
2. Partnership Relationship: Different company culture
3. Fundamentals: Scope of project
4. Management and Leadership: Information sharing
5. Management and Leadership: Running success criteria
These challenges were considered the most valuable to resolve.
The next step is to compare these challenges with the selected support elements from
Phase 2. The goal is to determine whether these elements address similar issues and
whether they can support companies beyond manufacturers and product owners, extend-
ing across the value chain.

4.3.2
Elements highlighted
The challenges identified during the workshop have been added to the element compari-
son matrix and are highlighted as yellow rows in fig. 4.18.
46
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:63==
• Wrongly scoping a project is categorized under the orchestrator function, as the
challenge stemmed from attempting to orchestrate too much at once.
• Resource allocation is placed under Net-value creation and Governance, manage-
ment, and operational structure, as the challenge involves either misplacing re-
sources or discovering additional, unforeseen resource needs.
• Differences in company culture are categorized under Organizational values and
Governance, management, and operational structure to reflect the impact of cultural
misalignment.
• Lack of information sharing is placed under Communication and Learning and Knowl-
edge Sharing, as the need for improvement was identified in both areas.
• Measuring success is categorized under Measures of success, as the challenge
involved the absence of clear success criteria and ongoing evaluation metrics.

of them. These are Element 2 and Element 6: the Two-Stage Ecosystem [16] and Inno-
