The strengths of these two support elements could serve as a foundation for further in-
vestigation in developing a framework for collaboration in the circular economy transition.
The element of stages
Both elements incorporate a timeline or stages of collaboration development.
These
stages help structure the tasks at hand and clarify what needs to be completed before
progressing to the next stage. However, they can also function as iterative phases with
key gateways, such as conducting a readiness assessment before implementing orches-
tration mechanisms [16].
The stages may also align with milestones related to product development, strategic im-
plementation in the market, or the formation of an innovation community [15].
A proposed framework for ecosystem transformation could follow a staged approach, as
illustrated in fig. 5.4.
Stage 1: Ecosystem Readiness Assessment
This phase involves evaluating ecosystem readiness, including:
• Business model assessment (value creation, capture, and delivery) [16].
• Environment assessment (industry trends, customer interest, technology, regulatory
factors) [16].
• Partners assessment (roles and responsibilities) [16] [15].
This structured approach may provide a more straightforward pathway for collaboration
and circular economy transition. The three assessments could be done as an iterative
process with a key gateway, such as milestones that need to be developed or a defined
promoter team.
Stage 2: Ecosystem Orchestration Mechanisms [16].
This stage focuses on establishing orchestration mechanisms to facilitate collaboration
and resource sharing. It includes:
• Standardization – Defining and legitimizing goals and guidelines [16].
• Nurturing – Allocating resources and developing competencies [16].
48
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:65==
• Negotiation – Enhancing collaboration and resource-sharing practices [16].
In addition to Ecosystem Orchestration Mechanisms, specific actions (collaboration mech-
anisms) can be done by different actors to overcome barriers. These actions often in-
volve communication, verification, or structuring processes.
A list of examples taken
from table 4.9[15] could be; Perspective, Power displacement, Synchronizing of circular
knowledge, Trusting, Advocating circularity, etc. The actors responsible for implementing
these collaboration mechanisms are referred to by various terms in the literature, includ-
ing promoters [15], facilitators, organization representatives [17], or orchestrators [16].
The actors could have roles of responsibility like the promoters, taken from table 4.7 [15];
Power(top-management), Expert(knowledge sharing structure), Relationship(innovation
partners), Process(organization structure), Universal(multiple roles and responsibility).
Stage 3: Operation and Maintenance [15]
This phase involves the established structure of an innovation community, which should
have been developed through stages 1. and 2., incorporating various promoter roles and
support mechanisms to ensure long-term success.
Figure 4.19: The three stages
The element of challenges
Through the stages, there are most likely challenges that need to be solved. The chal-
lenges could be like [15] structured into four spaces. The four spaces of challenges used
by [15] are: Individual, Firm, Value chain, and Linking partners fig. 4.20.
• The individual describes the barriers between the individual people in a team this
could be things like lack of knowledge or skills [15].
• The firm describes the barriers across the teams when trying to collaborate between
the different units inside an organization. This could be the teams working in silos
with a lack of alignment, or communication in between [15].
• For the value chain, the barriers are outside the company or organization and could
be the struggle of partnering, trusting, developing, and replacing to create the new
collaborative structure [15].
• The institutional barriers are often between the value chain partners and the gov-
ernment or regulations either they go together to overcome these barriers or they
divide the workload [15].
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition

==PAGE:66==
Figure 4.20: Four space of barriers

4.4
Phase 4: Identification of barriers for collaboration for CE

4.4.1
Planning
Development of search string
The focus of the systematic literature review is the identification and systematization of
barriers to collaboration for CE transition in the technical cycle.
The keywords have been developed with the focus of five criteria:
1. The papers have to have a main focus on the circular economy or circular economy
transition.
2. The collaboration in the papers has to be across the SC, VC, or in the ecosystem.
3. The paper has to contain an industry focus in the technical cycle.
4. The paper has to contain collaboration or the dynamic of the partners
5. The paper has to contain barriers or challenges in the collaboration.
The following keywords are chosen after testing
CE:
”circular”
Placement for collaboration:
”supply chain”
”value chain”
”ecosystem”
Technical cycle and industry:
”manufacturer”
”producer”
”industry”
Collaboration or collaborator:
”collaborator”
”partner”
”stakeholder”
”relationship”
”facilitator”
”orchestrator”
”enabler”
Barriers:
”barrier”
”boundary”
”challenge”
The search string is modified while analyzing if it is worth keeping a more restrictive or
broader search string depending on the relevance of the papers. This is done through iter-
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
51
==PAGE:68==
ations by testing the different placement of keywords, adding or removing keywords, their
spelling, and choosing the string that returns the most relevant papers. The chosen ad-
dition of an asterisk(*) to include multiple spelling or similar words is included in table 4.11.
Initially, a limit of papers from the years 2019-2024 was incorporated into the search string,
giving 70 articles. However, removing the limit only introduced 8 more articles. Deeming
that there were no good reasons for not including the last 8 in the search.
”Circular” was tested as both TITLE or KEY limitation. This gave two different groups of
articles. Where the latter included 20 more articles; however, when comparing the differ-
ent articles, the ones with ”circular” in the title were seen as more relevant to this study.
The final string used the 28/12/2024 in Scopus.
TITLE ( ”supply chain” OR ”value chain” OR ”ecosystem” )
AND
TITLE ( ”circular” )
AND
TITLE-ABS-KEY ( ”manufacturer*” OR ”producer*” OR ”industr*” )
AND
TITLE-ABS-KEY ( ”collaborat*” OR ”partner*” OR ”stakeholder” OR ”relationship”
OR ”facilitator” OR ”orchestrator” )
AND
TITLE-ABS-KEY ( ”barrier*” OR ”boundar*” OR ”challenge*” )

Inclusion and exclusion criteria
The inclusion and exclusion criteria are developed to define which articles are possible
candidates and which should be excluded, they can be seen in table 4.12.
For the inclusion criteria, the studies must present barriers, boundaries, or challenges
concerning collaboration. For the exclusion criteria, the studies collected must not focus
52

on the food chain or barriers concerning the implementation of technology.

IC-1

Extraction of barriers
The extraction requirements are developed to define which barriers to collect from the fi-
nal selection of papers. The barriers from the final selection of papers are solely collected
if they follow:
1. The barrier is well-defined and is described as hindering collaboration, partnership,
or the relationship between the different actors.
2. The barrier is well-defined and is suggested to be overcome by collaboration, part-
nership, or the relationship between the different actors.
This excludes barriers in the papers with other focus. An example could be barriers due
to the transition of CE or the implementation of new technology.
4.4.2
Execution
The search string is run in Scorpus, the 28/12/2024.
Selection of papers
The procedure for the selection of relevant studies is illustrated in fig. 4.21.
The publications are read and sorted using the following list from top to bottom:
• Filter 1: Read title, keywords
• Filter 2: Read abstract
• Filter 3: Read the introduction and conclusions
• Filter 4: Read the complete article.
If a publication at one point is deemed to fulfill EC-1 or EC-2 it is removed.
Filter 1. mainly removed publications with ”Title” or ”Keywords” containing ”Agriculture,”
”Bio-economy,” or ”Food.” Assuming they were focusing on the biological cycle (EC-2).
Filter 2. mainly removed publications lacking a description of collaboration from reading
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
53
==PAGE:70==
the abstract.
At filter 3. The publications left would all have a description of collaboration or barriers in
their abstract. Filters 3. and 4. mainly remove articles where the provided barriers did not
fulfill the inclusion criteria.
Figure 4.21: Illustration of the selection process
The search delivered 78 papers whereas the final selection of relevant studies contained
16 papers. One additional paper [16] was added to the collection from a backward search
of another study [28] included in the search. Giving a total of 17 papers. The full list of all
papers can be found in appendix E, and a snapshot of the document is shown in fig. 4.22.
Figure 4.22: Snapshot of included and excluded articles, appendix E
54
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:71==
Extraction
This stage aims to collect the barriers from the final selection of papers without relating
them to each other. This process is illustrated in fig. 4.23.
The barriers are extracted when following the extraction requirements.
Figure 4.23: Extraction process of barriers
First, the title, year, and DOI are copied from the selected papers into a new Excel book
creating sheet A, fig. F.1. As each paper is read, it is noted in Sheet A, along with rele-
vant criteria described in table 4.13. The papers are analyzed one at a time to ensure a
systematic extraction process.
In Sheet B, fig. G.1, all extracted barriers are collected, with each row assigned a unique
ID number. Every barrier is added to an individual row, ensuring that each barrier re-
mains distinct. The relevant criteria from table 4.13 are documented in separate columns,
allowing for a structured overview.
The description and context of each barrier vary across papers. In some cases, a barrier
may be framed as a root cause, a stopping factor, a conclusion, an influencing factor, or
a logical resolution. Capturing these different perspectives helps to enhance the under-
standing of why a particular issue is considered a barrier in each study.
If a criterion cannot be extracted from a study, it is left unanswered. Additionally, while
”Circular Aim” and ”Classification from Literature” are documented, they are not included
in the further investigation of the barriers to the risk of including bias. However, they
provide valuable insights into the context in which the barriers were identified.
A total of 224 individual barriers were collected, representing the raw data for further
analysis. In section 4.5, the characteristics of these collected barriers are explored in
more depth.

4.5
Phase 5: Systematization of barriers for collaboration for CE
transition
When you know what to overcome this barrier becomes opportunities.
In this phase, the 224 collected barriers for collaboration for CE transition are further
analyzed. This is done through a process of 3 steps.
1. Step. Determine shared characteristics between the barriers.
2. Step. Cluster barriers.
3. Step. Extract information from barriers
4.5.1
Determine shared characteristics
It is of interest to determine the shared characteristics between the barriers to classify and
map them. This is done through a process.
1. Step. Barriers are roughly grouped into groups of similar characteristics.
2. Step. Each group is revisited with a full overview of collected characteristics.
3. Step. In each group, the subgroups are created.
First, they are roughly grouped into groups of similar characteristics. This process is an
iterative process where several different characteristics are tested.
Second, each group is revisited with a full overview of the characteristics. Identifying if
they are correctly placed or should be moved from the primary characteristic.
Lastly in each group, subgroups are created.
Determining similar characteristics is done by creating a new Excel sheet containing; The
Source code, ID number, Barrier, Description, and Context from Sheet B (appendix G).
These are then printed onto A3 paper and cut out so each barrier is on an individual card,
60
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:77==
see fig. 4.30a. All the cards are placed into a pile and the barriers are read one by one.
The barrier was then categorized through the following process. If the cards barrier con-
cerns a common area with other cards they are placed on the same table in a row. The
common area is the overall topic the barrier describes and is further described as a theme.
A new theme is created if a barrier can’t be placed in any other themes. This is done until
all the cards are placed on the tables fig. 4.30b.
(a) Barrier cards
(b) Tables with cards
Figure 4.30: Cluster process
Before beginning step 2 all rows are piled up again. Now there are piles of barriers con-
cerning similar areas. Each pile has a theme. Each theme is written on a sticky note
and placed at the tables as headlines. The list of themes includes: Trust, Data, Com-
munication, Data as a resource, Transparency, Data exchange, Type of data parame-
ters, Resources, Competition, Cross-Sector, Pricing, Organization structure, Guidance,
Business Model, Management, Product, Scale, Standards, Regulations, CE strategies,
Stakeholder, Value proposition, Strategy, Alignment, Information exchange, Geographic,
Power, Data sharing, Consumer, Reverse Logistic, Ecosystem readiness, Return on in-
vestment, Investment cost, Commitment, Culture, Linear organization legacy, Standards,
Scoping, Knowledge and skills.
When placing the themes, it became clear that some of them were similar or subgroups
of an even bigger theme, for example, the theme ”Data,” which then included: Data as
a resource, Data, Data exchange, Type of data parameters, Information exchange, Data
sharing. They all shared the characteristic of information between partners in SC or VC.
When all the themes were structured the barriers were again one pile at a time read and
placed under the theme where the barrier had the strongest connection. The total list of
themes can be seen in table 4.15.

Reflection on the process
This process has been done over several iterations because of the complexity of the topic.
Several different characteristics have been tried to classify the barriers. These were again
disqualified because of the high number of assumptions required to place many of the
barriers. The assumptions could be due to a lack of information, the barrier not belonging
to the category, or the barrier belonging to multiple categories.
A characteristic tried was:
• Internal / External placement of barrier. Internal barriers occur inside the firm or
individually. External barriers occur in the VC or SC.
62
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:79==
• Individual, firm, value chain and linking partners criteria from [15].
• Circular strategy supported or strengthened by overcoming the barrier.
• Knowledge / Money / Time resource needed to overcome the barrier or used on the
barrier.
The process of developing the characteristics while reading the barriers compared to hav-
ing them preliminary chosen. Required a clear definition of what each theme included
when developed. This also resulted in the additional step 2. of revisiting them all.
4.5.2
Clustering of barriers
The printed barriers are then clustered, if they cover a similar challenge. An example
could be the six barriers in table 4.16, which all concern standards. Number 1, 109, and
190 all concern almost the same issue of lacking standards for performance assessment.
Therefore, they are clustered into ”Lack of standard system performance indicators con-
cerning: evaluating collaboration, management, and CE in SC.” and written into a new
sheet called Barrier matrix fig. 4.31. Giving a lower level of detail but a higher level of
systematic overview.
Barrier 36 and 59 are not clustered together because they do not focus on performance
assessment. These two are written into the matrix individually.
The barrier ID numbers given at the raw data collection (Sheet B fig. G.1) are kept in
the barrier matrix fig. 4.31. Making it possible to back-track the clustering, grouping, and
more details if necessary. In the Barrier matrix, the following criteria are documented: ID
number, Source, Barrier, Detail/Examples, Theme, Subgroup.

Reflection on the process
This process required a balance of the level of detail. The motivation for collecting the bar-
riers was to identify opportunities. It was also hoped to identify packages of opportunities.
If the level of detail is too high, the mapping could become too complex and muddying the
overview. If the level of detail is too low, there is the risk of losing the accurate picture of
the situation and the function of the mapping.
This process made it clear that the current situation is complex, and the barriers found
were not in just one specific area; they required multiple actors and roles.
64
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:81==
4.5.3
Interview
Four interviews were conducted with three companies to collect other barriers from the
industry. The barriers were collected through a semi-structured interview following the
interview guide described in appendix K. The interview was separated into three parts.
1. Part. Setting the scene by describing the objective, motivation, and goal of the
master’s thesis and barrier collection.
2. Part. Asking interview questions from the interview guide while adding follow-up
questions based on the conversation. The goal is to understand the companies’
current situations, collaboration, and experienced barriers without priming them with
material found in the literature.
3. Part. Showing an overview of themes from the barriers collection to collect additional
examples or context on barriers. The development of the overview is described in
the interview guide.
The barriers are mapped on individual sheets in the barrier matrix Excel, following the
same structure as the barriers described in section 4.5.2 see fig. 4.33. And then added
to the theme cards developed in the final stage. The result can be found in ??.
Additional information from interviews is structured in a separate sheet. Each column rep-
resents a characteristic, such as CE strategy, collaboration, orchestrator, governmental,
or customer. If an interview mentions a characteristic, a relevant quote is added. New
characteristics can be added as needed. This is solely to help maintain an overview of
what has been said in the interviews and to identify if any of the interviews mention sim-
ilar topics. Each cell contains only one quote and a new row is added to the company if
they have several quotes in the same topic fig. 4.34. All interviews key takeaways and
selected quotes are documented in appendix L, appendix M, appendix N, appendix O.

and focus areas.
”There will be more of that (collaborations) later. Right now, we have some sparring net-
works. In a way, we are innovating together. And in my experience, many people face
the same challenges. If you’re kind, offer a cup of coffee, and ask insightful questions,
most people are willing to help, I think.” (B. Q. 7)
Company C is the only B2C company selling one-time-use products through retailers
to private consumers. Two employees from this company were interviewed. One is a
specialist in the internal organization and has a lot of experience in the company’s sus-
tainability development. While the other is a specialist in their take-back program. This
company’s take-back program is in the scale-up phase.
”No one has challenged industry collaboration, aside from the fact that it comes with high
complexity. And there is a complexity to it. This is the next thing we need to address...”
(C2. Q. 35)

The theme cards are a front page featuring the main headline assigned to each barrier
fig. Q.1a. A large circle indicates the total number of barriers from the literature, while a
smaller number represents barriers identified from interviews. The theme cards provide
a broad overview with minimal detail but ensure a high-level overview to identify oppor-
tunities. On the subsequent pages, a table is created for each sub-criteria, including the
Headline on the theme card, barrier description, and sources that support the claimed
barriers and interviews. Some barriers are illustrated with examples written in italic. If
a barrier has an additional link to another theme, it is highlighted in red text. For those
seeking more details about the theme cards, the barriers can be arranged according to the
theme and sub-criteria in the barrier matrix appendix I, where examples are also included.
The developed theme cards can be found in full size in appendix Q including the table
of barriers belonging to each. The following text briefly introduces each theme, its main
barriers from interviews and literature, and its subcategories.

70
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:87==
Figure 4.39: Data
Data
Data is the theme car with most barriers and consists of all the barriers related to mea-
suring, collecting, and exchanging data and information flow. This also includes com-
munication, as collaboration requires data engagement (Company C). The data theme
comprises 30 unique barriers from the literature and six barriers identified in the inter-
views. All the interviewed companies face challenges with data. It is divided into seven
subcategories: Standards, Traceability, Visibility, Responsibility, Data exchange, Data
utilization, and Data governance.
Standards address the lack of consensus among stakeholders and the challenges of dif-
fering objectives or practices among them [28]. According to [32], this leads to increased
complexity in developing collaboration. Eleven different sources identified barriers within
this category. The most frequently mentioned barrier is ”Lack of a pre-defined standard
system for performance assessment.” This includes evaluating the performance of imple-
menting a circular strategy within supply chain (SC) or value chain (VC) collaborations
[31], [32], [29], [33], [34]. The absence of standardized circular indicators can create un-
certainty regarding whether the expected environmental benefits are realized following
modifications in stakeholders’ operations [35]. Various metrics and indicators, such as
material savings, energy, or water, are used in measuring sustainability. This presents a
challenge in determining which metric has the highest impact (Company A, B) and adds
to the complexity of stakeholders monitoring performance in different contexts [28], [32],
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
71
==PAGE:88==
[30], [36]. These factors are viewed as potential hindrances and complexities in develop-
ing collaboration [32].
Traceability addresses the absence of tracking for materials or products. The two most
frequently mentioned issues were ”Lack of material or product traceability, including ori-
gins, quality, lifecycle monitoring, and trust in shared information” [29] [33]. This leads to a
secondary impact on trust between partners and hampers performance assessment. The
second barrier is the lack of technology to enable data collection and exchange through-
out the entire supply chain, rather than just between two partners [30] [31]. Five different
sources identified barriers within this category.
Visibility encompasses the lack of transparency across the value chain. Unlike traceability,
which involves data on materials moving through the supply chain. The challenge lies in
the lack of transparency beyond the first or second tier of the value chain [30], or in the
insufficient data sharing both upstream and downstream, leading to an imbalanced level
of transparency [30]. It is primarily the original equipment manufacturers (OEMs) that
require data but fail to provide their own data in return [30]. The visibility challenge may
also stem from companies not understanding what data exists or could be collected within
the value chain, as they primarily focus on their own needs when measuring data. This
can result in stakeholders lacking data in certain areas of the value chain, unaware that
the data is actually being collected in other segments [35], [33]. Three different sources
highlight barriers in this area.
Responsibility involves the challenge of determining who is responsible for collecting what
data, what the requirements should be [30], and how the lack of data impacts the allocation
of responsibilities and the relationships between the suppliers [36]. Only two different
sources imposed barriers in this category.
Utilization addresses the lack of structure and how to use received data [35][30]. This is
the subcategory with the most barriers (9 unique). However, it was only collected from
7 individual sources. The challenges lie in how to utilize others’ data to promote circular
economy [35], getting the information to decision-makers at the right time [31], and being
able to store and manage it [36][34]. Company A observed that it took years for the
sustainability department to gather data before other parts of the organization recognized
its actual value.
Governance addresses the lack of frameworks and incentives for data gathering and shar-
ing [30], as well as the impact of GDPR on product and system lifetime data that could be
collected [35]. Only two distinct sources imposed barriers in this category.
Exchange addresses the insufficient flow of data [30], communication [37] [31], and infor-
mation [28] [35] [23]. This subcategory has six unique barriers sourced from nine distinct
references. The issues of delay and asymmetric communication and information shar-
ing within this category are also mentioned, potentially hindering supplier integration [37].
The limited sharing of knowledge and intellectual property [28] [35] [23], coupled with
data discrepancies and excessively strict specifications and regulations [35], contributes
to trust issues. Company C also highlighted it as a prerequisite, stating: “Knowledge
is often something companies are reluctant to share, but at the same time, it is the key
prerequisite for achieving truly great results.” (C1. Q. 23).
72
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:89==
Figure 4.40: Trust
Trust
Trust comprises all the barriers related to building or affected by trust. Five unique barri-
ers were identified from six different papers and two interviews. The most frequently men-
tioned barrier is ”Lack of trust in other actors or partners in the value chain”[30][35][29][33].
One argument is that the partnerships are young and have therefore not yet developed,
requiring third-party involvement [30]. The trust categories can be separated into barriers
of needing, having, and developing trust. Trust is needed for transparency [30][36] or
collaboration (Company A, C). Having trust in the value chain [30][35][29][33] and sectors
[35] is vital. Developing trust between companies that do not conduct business with each
other [35] is also essential. Trust is connected to data because the lack of trust affects
confidence in the data [30].
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
73
==PAGE:90==
Figure 4.41: Ecosystem
Ecosystem readiness
Ecosystem readiness comprises all barriers related to readiness of actors, supply chain,
frameworks, incentitives, strategies and technology. Ecosystem readiness are the second
biggest theme consisting of 17 barriers from the literature and 7 barriers from interviews.
It are split into three subcategories; Regulation and standardization, Frameworks and
theory and Industry.
Regulation and standardization address frameworks and incentives provided by govern-
mental stakeholders in the ecosystem, such as the EU or UN. There are nine barriers,
four of which stem from Company C. Seven papers discuss barriers in the context of
regulations. These barriers encompass deficiencies in the enforcement of regulations
[34][37][30], overregulation [30], lack of standardization [29],[36][38][30], and outdated
regulations [39][30]. This indicates that there are various perspectives on whether there
should be more or less regulation and whether it should be broader or more specific. Com-
pany C also faces the challenge of ensuring compliance with regulations like competition
laws while developing industry collaborations:
“One of the major challenges is when bringing competing companies together. We are
naturally extremely cautious to ensure that no issues arise regarding cartel formation or
any other legal concerns.” (C2 Q. 11),
74
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:91==
supported by [30] in the literature. Moreover, Company C identifies a lack of legislation or
mechanisms to support collaborative initiatives.
Frameworks and theory address theoretical knowledge, frameworks, and support within
the ecosystem. There are six barriers, two of which originate from interviews. Eleven
different papers highlight barriers in this subcategory. The barrier mentioned most fre-
quently is the inadequate awareness of circular economy principles among supply chain
partners and organizations [29], [34], [37], [31], [23], [38], and [36]. Company B also
identifies the challenge and necessity of choosing between the conceptual frameworks of
circular economy and eco-design.
”...There is a significant overlap between the two. But what we call things also matters in
terms of how we communicate and operationalize them...”(B Q.5).
This could indicate a lack of skills, knowledge, experience, training, awareness, and know-
how, aligning with the second most cited barrier: the absence of successful business mod-
els, practical guidance, and frameworks for implementing circular economy principles in
the supply chain and fostering a collaborative process [31], [39], [33], [37], [16]. Company
C also points out the lack of systemic mechanisms for fostering collaboration.
The industry addresses ecosystem challenges based on technology and infrastructure.
There are nine barriers identified, with only one stemming from an interview. These bar-
riers were gathered from ten different papers. The most commonly cited barriers, men-
tioned by four papers, are the lack of infrastructure and suppliers [29][33][23][36], where
insufficient investment in appropriate technology limits potential candidates for collabora-
tion [36]. The shortage of suppliers mentioned is primarily for circular processes such as
disassembly and recycling. This may suggest a shortage of value recovery companies,
as well as maintenance and repair firms, which were also mentioned by company C.
”Getting a recycling partner up and running in this field, which is a relatively new area, has
also been a challenge. Again, when it comes to value recovery companies—how many
are there, and can they build a viable business case around this? (C2. Q. 14).
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
75
==PAGE:92==
Figure 4.42: Organization
Organization
Organization includes all barriers related to the structure and culture of an organization.
There are nine barriers along with two identified from the interviews. Organizations are
divided into two subcategories: organizational structure and culture, and organizational
resistance.
The organization structure and culture pose challenges in transitioning to a circular econ-
omy due to a lack of flexibility [37] [36], insufficient collaboration across departments [36],
inadequate readiness assessments [16], and the time required to create awareness and
foster a mindset within the organization (Companies A, B). Another challenge involves
the differing cultures and structures between organizations in cross-sector collaborations,
with varying logics, belief systems, and behaviors [32].
Organizational resistance to transitioning has the main barrier of lack of willingness/re-
luctance to change [32][36][34][28] or being bound to the current business model [40][30]
and a linear incentive system that favors linear targets [39][35]. The incentive system is
viewed as a management decision that hinders the organization and gives a connection
to management.
76
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:93==
Figure 4.43: Management
Management
Management encompasses all barriers related to internal organizational management and
external management of collaboration. Management is therefore divided into two cate-
gories: Internal and External. There are 11 unique barriers identified in the literature and
2 from interviews.
Internal management involves six barriers identified across ten different papers. Three
of these barriers focus on willingness and readiness, while the other three emphasize
planning and risk. The primary barrier is a lack of support and commitment from top man-
agement [23][31][32][34][29][36][37]. This barrier can narrow the organization, limiting its
potential for collaboration [32], and is also identified as a necessity for implementing a
circular strategy in the supply chain [36]. Company C supports this view, mentioning it as
a starting point for initiating collaboration:
”You need to have your top management with you. You must anchor what you do in top
management - so that is where we start.” (C2 Q.20).
The second most frequently mentioned barrier was a lack of knowledge and experience
regarding circular economy concepts, business opportunities and vision [41][34][32].
External barriers across collaboration identified six barriers across six different papers
and one interview, which revealed one new barrier and reinforced another. The most
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
77
==PAGE:94==
frequently mentioned barrier was the challenge of developing a coordination plan due to
the complexity of operating and monitoring the supply chain [23][17][16]. This barrier was
also acknowledged by Company C, which also indicated a lack of experts in orchestrating
or managing partnerships.
”It’s also worth considering whether there’s a need for more professional expertise to
manage these partnerships so that not everyone who needs to learn it. Because it is a
discipline in itself...” (C1 Q. 12)
Other barriers included differences among the actors in their practices and risk manage-
ment approaches [30][32] and limited transparency due to fear of sharing proprietary in-
formation [37].
Figure 4.44: Commitment
Commitment
Commitment involves all barriers associated with the level of intent in collaboration and
implementing circular strategies.
There are eight barriers to commitment, two identi-
fied through interviews. Eight different papers discuss these barriers. The most men-
tioned barriers are the lack of willingness and acceptance of new circular business models
among stakeholders [29][33][36]. It is also noted that there is a dilemma regarding which
of the multiple circular strategy initiatives to prioritize (Company A) or the different level
of intent among actors in the supply chain [36] or reducing the intent to simplify collabo-
78
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:95==
ration with anyone other than the closest or most trusted partner in the value chain [39].
Company C also mentions partnership as a long-term resource commitment, which can
be a barrier for those companies that cannot afford it.
Figure 4.45: Competition
Competition
Competition involves barriers hindering collaboration due to the market competition. There
are five unique barriers, and one from an interview. The barriers come from five different
papers. The most mentioned barrier is the lack of transparency and information sharing,
from the fear of losing market opportunities [32] [29] [30] or competitive advantage among
actors in the supply chain if they can calculate resale value in advance [35]. Company C
also mentions the uncertainty surrounding the confidentiality barrier in collaboration with
competitors and how the perception of it is a barrier as well:
” One of the major barriers can be confidentiality. How do you collaborate with a competi-
tor? How do you share information in a way that doesn’t harm your own production?”...
”But how do you prevent it from being perceived as a barrier and instead create a process
where everyone can fully engage and contribute in the best possible way to achieve the
highest level of circularity—or whatever the goal may be?”(C1 Q. 21)
This is also supported by [32] mentioning that competitions create tension among collab-
orators leading to frustration and violation of ”true spirit”.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
79
==PAGE:96==
Figure 4.46: Economic
Economic
Economic has four barriers from the literature mentioned in 9 different papers and three
from interviews. Economic involves barriers hindering collaboration due to the lack of eco-
nomic attractiveness and uncertainty on return of investment [31][29][33][34][30][42][37].
This can be due to the fear of not being able to manage the supply chain properly [37],
the unstable prices of secondary resources [37][31] or the limited financial resources and
funding [34][23]. Company C mentions the shortage of long-term resources to orchestrate
the collaboration effectively. Where Company A mentions the uncertainty in net-value
creation and ownership,
”There isn’t necessarily an existing system in place. So, there is also a certain level of
mutual risk or a shared investment along the way. Who owns the outcome? ... What
is the value for us? Do we have an interest in owning this system? What’s in it for us?
What’s in it for you? When is the price right?... what is net-value?” (A. Q. 16)
80
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:97==
Figure 4.47: Power structure
Power structure
Power structures are only mentioned in two papers, each presenting its own unique bar-
rier. From the interviews, two companies agreed on one common barrier, bringing the total
to three barriers. One of the barriers from the literature is asymmetric resources in organi-
zations [32]. Where the other is asymmetric market power due to many small companies
and few large suppliers [39]. Both companies interviewed recognized the asymmetric
power from economies of scale when collaborating with their suppliers, strengthening the
first barrier.
“…I think we’re missing some economy of scale to be able to change much.”... ”That’s just
natural. And as I see it, we simply don’t have a large enough critical mass. Our suppliers
are big enough that they have other, larger customers than us.” (B Q. 9.)
“… Even though we see ourselves as a large company, for many suppliers, we are just a
small and insignificant player. But when we unite as an industry, we often share the same
suppliers, and that allows us to exert greater pressure.”(C1 Q. 11)
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
81
==PAGE:98==
Figure 4.48: Scale
Scale
The scale consists of three barriers mentioned in only three papers from the literature;
however, there are also three identified in the interviews. The main challenges are the
lack of scalability due to the need for stakeholder collaboration [39] and the uncertainty
associated with scaling up:
”…We are in a pilot phase now, but when we scale up—that’s important. Can we scale
up without breaking the bank?” (A. Q. 19)
For the company in the scaling phase, there was the challenge of how to scale and how
to establish a long-term solution within the value chain.
”That’s the challenge we’re facing—how do you scale? It’s not an art to run a small pilot
project in one or two cities. The real challenge is scaling it into something with a significant
impact—something that becomes an integrated part of the entire value chain. That’s what
we are working on.” (C2 Q. 26) And then there were how to structure and standardize the
scaling phase also mentioned by company C.
82
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:99==
Figure 4.49: Alignment
Alignment
Alignment focuses on the challenge of alignment between collaborative partners. There
are six barriers from 10 different papers and two from the interviews. The main barrier are
the lack of shared vision [36][34][29][33],[28][37][32] or misalignment or strategic priorities
and business model [36]. The versatility of the ecosystem is also cited as a challenge
in finding mutual value opportunities [39]. There is also a misalignment of capacities
and capabilities, which, according to [36], is often realized in the implementation phase.
Additionally, there is the alignment of roles and responsibilities, both mentioned by [16]
and Company A.
4.5.5
Barrier connection
When reading the context and description of the barriers, it was noticed that some barriers
had a second effect, which also connected with one of the other themes. This connec-
tion was documented when clustering the barriers in the barriers matrix from appendix I
where each barrier got a number 4 if the connection was deemed valid. This connection
has now been drawn, giving the following diagram fig. 5.6. Where Data standard was
linked to trust through the ”Lack of verification mechanism for reliability of self-declared
data” [30]. The absence of verification has a secondary effect on trust between part-
ners. In data traceability, the ”Lack of material or product traceability, including origins,
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
83
==PAGE:100==
Figure 4.50: Connection between barriers
quality, lifecycle monitoring, and trust in shared information” [29] [33]. This leads to a
secondary impact on trust between partners and hampers performance assessment. In
data exchange: data discrepancies and excessively strict specifications and regulations
[35], contribute to trust issues. Trust is also connected to data because the lack of trust
affects confidence in the data [30]. Competition was also connected to data due to the
lack of transparency because of market competition [35], [30],[32]. Competition was con-
nected by management due to limited transparency and willingness from management
[37]. Management is connected by economic due to the uncertain financial cost manage-
ment of circular supply chain, which is highlighted as important [37]. Organizations are
also pointing at management due to the barrier of linear incentive systems and institu-
tional conditions set by the management are a barrier in the organization, slowing down
the implementation of circular economy [39][35][36]. Management points to commitment
because they lower their priorities of implementing circular economy to other things like
production, expansion, or market share [31]. Commitment points on alignment due to the
collaboration partners can have misaligned their commitment level of implementation of
circular economy [36]. Alignment connects with trust because of the lack of shared vision,
trust and fear of losing control are all mentioned in the same combination [34][29][33].
The last one also points at trust in organizations because the organization’s resistance to
change is also due to a lack of trust [36][34].
These connections found are not supported enough to say how the connection should be.
And there could be different connections depending on the situation or types of collabo-
ration. However, in the interviews, the interviewees mentioned things in specific orders
and used phrasing like: ”firstly, these are done..” and ”after this, it is possible to”. This
could indicate that there could be a better or worse structure of how and when to handle
the different barriers. However first the barriers should be seen as opportunities.
84
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:101==
5
Discussion
The developed theme cards from phase 5 and the support elements from phase 3 have
been discussed and reflected on in this chapter, along with the research questions.
5.1
Phase 6: Mapping opportunities
5.1.1
From barrier to opportunity
The theme cards developed in Phase 5 can be seen as representing key building blocks
for circular collaboration. Each block gives insights into actions, and critical stages requir-
ing attention. These cards document barriers and act as opportunity triggers, illustrating
where targeted interventions could support collaboration for circular economy transition
in the value chain.
Figure 5.1: From barrier to opportunity
It is under the connection that understanding and overcoming barriers can actively create
opportunities. A suggestion could be to systematically flip each barrier into a correspond-
ing opportunity, thereby allowing companies to build structured capability maps that can
help them clarify current capabilities and desired future competencies. This approach
builds on concepts from orchestration literature, such as the orchestration mechanics
proposed by Parida et al.[16] and collaboration mechanics described by Hansen [15].
It could also integrate with other capabilities mentioned in the literature like dynamic ca-
pabilities(human, knowledge, leadership, culture, mindset, management strategy, opera-
tional strategy, resilience etc.) [36] of management strategies (reducing & coping, training
& education, performance indicators, tools & digitalization, pricing strategy, risk manage-
ment etc.) [37], cross-sector strategies [32]. Other papers brought enablers like techno-
logical aspects from industry 4.0 (digital transform process, remote assistance and guid-
ance, system integration and interoperability) [38] or data sharing structures to promote
different solutions [35].
These maps could offer a structured way to create awareness and guide decision-making,
helping companies assess where they stand today and where they need to improve their
collaborative capabilities.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
85
==PAGE:102==
Figure 5.2: Capability maps
A capability map could help companies identify capability gaps — indicating where invest-
ment is needed in skills, technology, or processes. It could also function as a guide for
structured collaboration, helping companies align their collaboration efforts by highlighting
shared priorities, potential risks, and weaknesses. For example, the challenges related to
data sharing, performance measurement, and cross-company data utilization were found
to be central barriers across the companies interviewed.
Data - an example
Data can serve as an example of how to use the capability map based on the collected
barriers fig. 5.3.
• For the identified practical gap relates to data standardization [35]. There could be
a developed, standardizing format and data protocols [16] [37].
• To reduce information asymmetry and lack of sustainability assessment, there could
be proposed information-sharing schemes and five performance indicators [35] work-
ing as common proof points. The aim is that decisions are made based on shared,
transparent goals, with all actors optimizing toward the same parameters or targets.
This was also used by one of the interviewees: ”With four companies, we entered
the market together, creating a joint initiative to solve this challenge collectively.
We identified five proof points to work on, demonstrating that an industry-solution
and collaboration are the right things to do, more than individual companies doing it
alone. And so far, we have been quite successful in achieving this.” (C2 Q. 5).
• Knowledge on new technologies such as product passports could further facilitate
data sharing and transparency across the value chain [30].
• Setting clear guidelines when having meetings and discussions: ”We have actually
built a high level of trust, allowing us to have good discussions on these matters.
86
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:103==
However, when it comes to anything related to marketed products, competition in
the market, and pricing, we do not discuss these topics at all. We have clear guide-
lines in place for every meeting, ensuring that these subjects are off-limits. Meeting
agendas are set, and minutes are taken. Naturally, there are important considera-
tions when collaborating with direct competitors.” (C2 Q. 13)
Figure 5.3: Capability map focus points
However, this approach remains conceptual and requires further validation. Future work
could involve pilot studies where companies apply the capability mapping process in real-
life collaborations, mainly to test its adaptability across different industries and collabora-
tion types in a PS-phase.
5.1.2
Stages
From the support element for collaboration stages of the implementing and building pro-
cess was defined fig. 5.4.
Figure 5.4: The three stages
An insight from the interviews is the importance of structuring collaboration into clear
stages and analyzing support elements in Phase 3 [16][15]. All companies described
similar initial steps when initiating their circular transition. One company also suggested,

if it were possible, to place the themes in sequence.
”... perhaps it can also be se-
quenced. I try to consider: How do you initiate it? How do you structure the discussion?
How do you work towards a clear end goal in what you are doing?” (C2 Q. 30) The steps
all the companies described were:
• Collecting data - To establish a baseline, understanding the areas of impact, and
strategy development. Enabling fact-based discussions where they are speaking
with data.
• Knowledge sharing - By developing training material and guidelines.
• Top management support - Ensure the the top management are onboard.
There are a indication that there could be developed a structure of how these building
blocks can be placed in order as illustrated in fig. 5.5. It could be a sequence the building
block could be placed or followed. Maybe one building block has to be fully developed as
a key before initiating the second.

This could suggests that collaboration could benefit from a sequenced model. It could be
where certain elements must be in place before others can succeed. Or the connection
could maybe also not be generic but specific from company to company or collabora-

Parida [16].

tions of arrows between the building block and then identify where they have the biggest

These interlinks are solely an indication, building on the context of the barriers from the
structured literature review and the interviews. There has been no methodology used in
the weighing of the connection. For robustness of the study, this would require further
investigation with the stages of developing a partnership as the focus.
Future work could explore whether such a sequencing guide could be generalized across
industries, or in different collaboration types (value chain, industry, cross-sector).
5.2
The role of the orchestrator
The orchestrator role was highlighted in both the literature and empirical data. The com-
plexity of circular collaborations, particularly those including multiple companies or even
competing firms. The complexity was also found as an unavoidable consequence be-
cause of various added operations [38] or that to enable circular strategy of recycling and
reverse logistic there has to be developed a sector or industry solution (company C,A).
Requiring company C and A to work with competing firms or a network of firms
According to Hansen, [15] collaboration can be split into cooperation and coordination.
Cooperation as the motivation and commitment of the collaboration with the pursuit of a
shared goal and benefits, with contributing. Coordination is on the other hand describing
the structure and process of collaboration with the aim of aligning actions and facilitating
information sharing [15]. According to Hansen, does a higher uncertainty need more
extensive coordination [15]. This coordination role is also described in other literature as
the orchestration role [16].
This need for a central orchestrator to manage alignment, coordination, and progress
tracking resulted in company C taking the orchestrator role itself, driving collaborative in-
novation, building commitment and trust, data sharing, and business model development.
The company tells that the role of orchestrating is a key success factor for the collaboration
to continue and the strategy to be implemented.
”Right now this is what we orchestrate, we are driving innovation, developing business
models, building commitment and trust, and organize ourselves around these efforts. We
are also collecting and utilizing data, so we talk with data and so on. This is actually
what we orchestrate right now, and we do it in a period to drive this to get things moving.
However, to make it sustainable in the long run, there needs to be a separate structure
where these activities can be.” (C2 Q. 36)
However, company C also emphasizes the need of a separate structure. Company A, not
acting as orchestrator themselves, relied on a third-party orchestrator who had a network
portfolio of suppliers. Having the orchestrator as an external facilitator rather than one
from the collaborating companies. The value recovery company from the initial workshop
also took the orchestration role as a value chain partner. This was not for the sector
or industry collaborations, but for the value chain and cross-value chains collaboration.
Another value recovery company, Stena Recycling, has developed Stena Consulting [19],
helping companies orchestrate their new networks as a mediator or facilitator. Company
C and A both state that they are not value recovery companies, so they don’t expect
to keep the role as orchestrator. This could indicate a link between orchestration and
value recovery companies - when the circular strategy includes reverse logistics. A car
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
89
==PAGE:106==
company, Renault, has developed a holding company including different small companies
with a focus on the different stages in the closed loop of an electric car. This holding
company works as a legal orchestrator.
This opens up an interesting business opportunity: the development of independent or-
chestration entities that act as neutral facilitators. These entities could help with:
• Compliance with laws and regulations. Especially when it is collaboration between
competitors.
• Alignment of goals, process and data-sharing
• Neutral facilitation of trust-building and conflict resolution.
The combination of all this could indicate that there is a value proposition for a standalone
orchestration service, especially when the collaborations are between competing or cross-
sector actors.
5.3
Types and dynamics of collaboration for circular economy
transition
The research identified the three collaboration types that companies engage in for circu-
lar economy transition. These three types were mentioned several times and included
different dynamics.
• Value chain collaboration - Includes direct collaboration between value chain actors.
• Sector/Industry collaboration - Includes collaboration between competitors.
• Cross-sector collaboration - Includes collaboration of actors with different cultures,
logics, and incentives.
Each type brings different dynamics and challenges. For value chain collaboration, exist-
ing customer-supplier relationships often provide a starting point for trust and data sharing
[35]. For sector collaboration, competitors must overcome concerns around intellectual
property and competition law, requiring clear governance structures [30]. For cross-sector
collaboration, partners face cultural and knowledge asymmetries, requiring additional ef-
fort in aligning expectations, value proposition and creating a common language [32].
A recurring theme across all collaboration types was the importance of trust, transparency
and commitment — all frequently mentioned in the literature and highlighted in all inter-
views. Transparency, particularly through data sharing, emerged as a key enabler of trust
[35].
The support elements from the literature had an increased focus on the commitment to
collaboration depending on the chosen strategy or business model [17] [27] [20]. The level
of collaboration intent and ownership dynamics [20] can turn out to be some of the main
focus companies have to align before starting a collaboration. As company C mentioned,
are the complexity changing, whether it is a value chain or a sector partnership.
”And this can become even more complicated if you have to collaborate with competitors...
It could be partnerships with those who are aligned on a shared mission. But then there
are also those who are used to not sharing anything at all—let alone collaborating—who
are suddenly expected to work together instead of competing against each other. So,
there are many different dynamics in partnerships.” (C1. Q. 1)
90
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:107==
5.4
Conclusion
This thesis initially focused on exploring collaboration within the value chain to support
the transition to a circular economy. The aim was to develop a generic, initial form of
support to guide such collaborations. However, as the research progressed, it became
evident that value chain collaboration is not a single, uniform concept. Instead, there
are multiple distinct types of collaboration, each with different characteristics, challenges,
and dynamics depending on factors such as the position in the value chain, the industries
involved, and the specific circular strategies being pursued.
A finding was that the responsibility for enabling and driving circular collaborations is often
placed on the orchestrator. This is largely due to the complexity and resource demands
associated with initiating and managing these partnerships. The literature similarly em-
phasizes the orchestrator’s role, and existing collaboration frameworks were found to tar-
get orchestrators, offering support in three key areas primarily: The first one was ”De-
cision support” for selecting the appropriate circular business model or strategy based
on the intent of the collaboration. The second was ”Guidance” on the stages and pro-
cess for implementing the collaboration. The third was ”Descriptions of collaboration”
and orchestration mechanics, such as governance structures, communication protocols,
and decision-making processes. The most frequently supported areas were the ”Deci-
sion support” followed by the ”Descriptions of collaboration,” with only one ”Guidance”
support, which was Paridas [16]. It was however found relatively close to also being a
”Descriptions of collaboration”.
The dynamics of collaboration were consistently framed around the importance of trust,
commitment, and transparency. These factors were seen as fundamental enablers for
successful collaboration, especially when partners had divergent interests or competing
priorities.
A range of challenges were identified through both literature and empirical data, which
could be grouped into the following categories: Data, Trust, Ecosystem Readiness, Orga-
nization, Management, Commitment, Competition, Economic, Power, Scale, Alignment,
and Standards. Among these, ”Data” emerged as the most critical challenge, serving
as both a barrier and a potential enabler for overcoming many of the other challenges.
This finding was strongly supported by the industry representatives, who highlighted data
sharing and standardization as their first priority in circular collaborations.
Additionally, the nature of the challenges shifts depending on the type of collaboration. In
competitor collaborations(industry collaboration), trust becomes a top priority. In cross-
sector collaborations, the organizational differences and cultural had an effect ass well.
The proposed support concept developed in this thesis focuses on reducing collabora-
tion complexity by mapping goals, expectations, and anticipated challenges early in the
process, while simultaneously enhancing transparency between actors. The most critical
challenges often arise at the beginning of collaborations, when uncertainty is high, and
the orchestrator typically addresses these.
Finally, the research found that orchestrators themselves exist in different forms — they
can be manufacturing companies, value recovery companies, independent consultancy
firms specialized in orchestration, or legally established entities such as holding compa-
nies. This diversity in orchestrator roles suggests a potential need for further research into
what needs there are in a support tool — particularly if the role of orchestration evolves
into a standalone business model in its own right.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
91
==PAGE:108==
5.5
Reflection and further work
5.5.1
Further work
The comprehensive DS-I developed in this thesis suggests how the findings could be used
to develop support for collaboration, but the complexity of the field makes it difficult to
conclude whether the proposed support fully addresses all relevant aspects. Due to time
constraints, this study was limited to empirical data collected from four companies — one
value recovery company and three product manufacturers. This narrow sample presents
a limitation, as only two value chain partners were involved in the empirical phase.
Future research could benefit from expanding the empirical data collection to include other
value chain actors, maintenance and repair services, product retailers, sub suppliers, and
logistic providers, to gain a more holistic perspective. Additionally, broadening the scope
across multiple industries would help in providing insights into whether there are more
general patterns and where collaboration opportunities lie.
It would also be valuable to explore how collaboration dynamics shift depending on the
type of collaboration — for example, whether the collaboration takes place within the value
chain, across different sectors, or within the same industry.
Future work could also benefit from applying alternative methods, such as cross-sector
or cross-value chain workshops that bring together different actors to co-create a support
for collaborative solutions.
Longitudinal studies, where the development of collaborations is tracked over time, could
also provide a better understanding of how different building blocks evolve and interact
throughout the process. Investigating how and if the importance of the different stages
in the collaboration process changes depending on this context, to help tailor the support
more effectively.
5.5.2
Reflection
Throughout this thesis project, I developed several academic and research-related skills,
particularly in conducting systematic literature reviews. The second literature review fol-
lowed a more structured process compared to the first, due to my progression in under-
standing both the field and the methodology. Reflecting on the first review, I recognize
that improvements could have been made in the design of the search string and the se-
lection criteria for papers. If the project were to be repeated, the search strategy would
have a higher focus on literature related specifically to collaboration support and tools,
rather than just the broader context.
This research aimed to develop a general form of support that could be applied across
industries, without targeting a specific industry or layer in the value chain. However, due
to the heavy representation of manufacturers in both the literature and the empirical data,
the resulting support can carry a degree of bias toward the manufacturing sector. Because
both the academic and empirical focus is skewed toward one type of actor.
Overall, this thesis has provided valuable learning not only in terms of academic research
processes but also in understanding the complexities of collaborative processes in circular
value chains. It has strengthened my ability to critically assess methodologies, handle
empirical data, and reflect on the implications and limitations of my own work.

Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition

6
Met Learning Objectives
Analysis
• Investigate, describe, and present different barriers, risk and failures that may
occur when implementing value chain collaborations for circular economy
transition. Through the phase 4 of this thesis, barriers are collected and analyzed
from the literature. The literature has collected the barriers from many different ac-
tors in the value chain, giving a total of 224 barriers. After analyzing and clustering
of the barriers, a total of 130 barriers were structured into theme cards, where most
of the risks were highlighted.
• Investigate, describe, and present different collaboration dynamics for CE. Dynam-
ics were found and discussed throughout both phase 3 and 5. They were compared
to different collaboration types(value chain, sectorial, and cross-sector) and a part
of the understanding and discussion for the developed support usage and value.
• Define relevant knowledge within the field of CE in relation to linear economy and
sustainability. In phase 1 of the research clarification, the dynamics, terms, and
different frameworks describing circular economy were presented. Giving an insight
into the complexity and diversity of circular economy transition.
Design & Evaluation
• Develop an overview of Opportunities for value chain collaboration for CE transition.
The capability map gives an overview of where to determine strength and opportu-
nities. The developed overview of opportunities is a combination of phase 5 and 6.
In phase 5, a run-through of the placement and structure of the main challenges is
made. Understanding and knowing the placement of the typical pitfalls helps under-
stand the opportunities which are possible in phase 6. Mapping the opportunities in
the capability map.
• Develop an initial support that guides the establishment of collaboration in CE tran-
sition. The capability map in combination with the stages of iteration for when a
company should revisit and update the map to keep the target, and be able to guide
where the strength and opportunities are in collaboration for circular economy tran-
sition. The guiding phase is the stages of iteration and sequence, which the building
blocks can be placed.
• Identify future research areas enabling collaboration for CE transition. Future re-
search areas are developed and described in the future research. Here, different
methodological types of research and focus areas are mentioned.

Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:119==
B
Initial Study
Initial research and data collection was done to develop search terms and conduct a
workshop in the case company. This was done through three different actions; Preliminary
literature search, visiting LOOP Forum 2024 https://www.loopforum.dk/, and meeting with
key stakeholders in the case company.
Development of workshop
Participants
These were determined by the distribution of knowledge in the company. Having the
participants responsible for stakeholders, partnerships, and business innovation.
Scope
The workshop was scoped to focus on the case company’s ecosystem with the aim of
identifying and understanding the interaction with key actors in the ecosystem. This it
described as key flows.
Framework
The framework for the workshop was the ”Ecosystem Map” from Ready2LOOP toolbox
[43] with modified cards to fit the company’s interactions and preliminary experience.
The cards were developed to help map the interaction between the stakeholders The first
three cards: Challenge, Dependency, and Information focused on the type of relationship
there was between the stakeholders.
The second three cards: End-Of-Production, End-Of-Use, and End-Of-Life focused on
the placement of the product-interaction compared to the placement of the loop in the
technical side of the butterfly diagram, see figure ??.
The bottom three cards: Regeneration Quality, Material (Quality and Amount), and Pro-
duct/Portfolio focused on value generation of the relationship between the stakeholder
and the recovery firm.
To investigate who or what was the initiator of a partnership an arrow was made in the
initiator’s direction. A red dot symbolizes the ”Motivation starting point” to illustrate the
reason for initiation. To understand what was a success for a partnership or the company
a green dot symbolizes the ”Success Measure Point”.
Execution
The company did not have a previous ecosystem map so the following steps were exer-
cised;
1. Identification of relevant stakeholders
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
103
==PAGE:120==
Figure B.1: Workhop cards
104
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:121==
2. Define the boundary of the ecosystem. Here government, legislation, and investors
were excluded.
3. Define the interactions between the actors of the ecosystem. Here the cards were
used.
4. Discuss and reflect on the ecosystem. Here the participants were asked if they saw
any new patterns in their ecosystem. Or saw some engagement with stakeholders
they could do differently?
Results from workshop
The case company is a Northern value recovery company that focuses on recirculating at
material levels. This can be discarded products, manufacturing waste, or post-consumer
when the product no longer can be reused. The company specialize in plastic, and the
sorting service of separating different types of plastic from each other. The company are
versatile with many types of plastic and are therefore working across different industries
and sectors.
The companies eco-system was mapped through a workshop to identify different stake-
holders, sectors and structures. The company were build on circular economy principle,
as one of the new players in the value chain. Its collaboration mechanics are specially
interesting compared to companies build on the linear principle, who need the transition to
circular economy. Through the workshop three network positions were detected; closed-
loop, open-loop, and institutions/academic research, see figure B.2. With the closed-loop
the company delivers a transformational development to the ecosystem.
Collaboration
Two motivational starting points for the creation of new relationships were found as; ”skills
and knowledge” and ”academic validation”.
With the company’s skills and knowledge from previous experience. The company acted
as a path-maker, educator, and match-maker for the actors in the ecosystem. Especially
actors who were working with the transition to CE. Alignment of strategies and needs were
highlighted as very important for a successful partnership. Making the value recovery
taking the function of orchestrating.
The academic validation of previous collaborations, tests or research that the company
had done enhanced their credibility for companies needing tests and joint co-creation of
new circular solutions. However a one of the main challenges for the company was the
lack of readiness, skills, or know-how from the company in re-introducing the recycled
material back into the company, leading to open-loop solutions.
It was found that the value recovery company has different roles in the position of the three
different networks. In general, the company has the orchestrating role in the closed-loop
and open-loop, managing information, match-making, and pushing business innovation.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
105
==PAGE:122==
Closed-loop
The company was founded and developed on the idea of closed-loop manufacturing. This
is where the company provides a service for mainly plastic converters (material sourcing),
brand owner, and manufacturing enabling the companies to recirculate their material from
their production waste or post-consumer to a new product. The company never owns the
material in the closed loop but provide the service. The material recovery enable the firms
who is using the company as a supplier to develop new design strategies and business
models, or optimize their production with less waste material.
The value creation is recycled mono-material which the firms can reintroduce to their own
product by substituting some of the virgin material.
Open-loop
It is not always possible for the manufacturer to reuse their material, this can be because
of legislation, mechanical properties, or lack of skill-set and knowledge. However, their
controlled waste streams, can be sorted into mono-materials utilized by another company,
introducing and industrial symbiosis.
The receiving company could be from the same sector or a completely different one.
Open-loop is seen as the less circular solution, why it is more seen as the last resort or
an enabler for the manufacturing company and legislation to evolve. The match-making
of the upstream and downstream is done by the value recovery company.
The value creation is recycled mono-material which can be utilized by another industry or
firm substituting some of the virgin material.
Academic Research, Legislation and institutions
The company participates in different collaborations with institutions and networks advo-
cating academic research. This could be assessment labs, certification body or material
providers. Especially the closed-loop model network requires a certifying and entrusting
of the product using the recovered material as a substitute minimizing the volume of virgin
material.
Key actors
The key actors found were; manufacturing companies, brand owners, plastic converters,
and academic partners e.g. universities, certification body, assessment labs.
For the actors the company worked as a business innovations partner, conducting tests
and trials together with the actors to co-create a solution.
Key flows
The key flows were material and information. The most used card in the workshop was
the information card. Every actor has a high amount of information flow, by speaking with
106
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:123==
Figure B.2: Material recovery company’s placement in value chains
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
107
==PAGE:124==
data.
With the matchmaking function of the open-loop solutions the company, is ensuring co-
ordination between different stakeholders in the network.
In the closed-loop solution, the company did not only have contact with the nearest stake-
holders like manufacture or material sourcing but also the distribution logistics and sales
and retail. Especially information, experience, skill-set, and data validation, when orches-
trating the co-created solution were a key flow between the company and all the actors of
the ecosystem.
Readiness of manufacturing firms
It was found that a certain initial readiness has to be assessed in the firm. Making the
recycling company work as a general assessor or consultant before becoming a supplier
or partner. The firms had barriers needing to be resolved before enabling the value chain.
This were both individual and on firm level barriers.
An individual barrier was mainly understanding the perspective and possibility of CE and
value of recycled/reintroduced material.
A firm barrier was mainly synchronizing the knowledge between the actors of the firm. This
could be knowledge about their waste handling ass is, developing of internal goals, limits,
resources and motivation. Waste has often been neglected and therefore documented in
a less detailed level as other processes in the production, resulting in a low knowledge
about, volume, quantity and quality of waste streams. This process may be developed or
initiated together with the case company.
A Value chain barrier were that Production and manufacturing cite are developed after lin-
ear approach and optimization. Making the infrastructure a challenge for implementation
of waste management or initial waste deposit places for initial sorting. Controlled waste
streams was seen as a barrier needed to be resolved before a partnership or collaboration
could be a possibility.
Design strategies and business model development
The material recovery enable the firms to develop new design strategies and business
models. It was observed that the manufacture companies mainly develop a new branch
in their production using the recovered material. This could be for a lower aesthetic re-
quirement product where e.g. the color does not matter.
Partnership or supplier
In the case company partnerships are seen as having a higher impact on decisions and
being dynamic with the manufacture/brand owner. This sets the company in a better
market position with the others company’s partnership as a brand reference for quality.
However a partnership requires the case company to allocate resources into develop-
ment together with the manufacture/brand owner e.g. for new business models, design
strategies, test different step-ups, and even facilitate or orchestrate the partnership struc-
ture and communication. The allocation on resources in collaborative innovation is a long
108
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:125==
term investment without a direct value capture, because the partnership is often about the
transitioning and innovation of CE models.
If the company takes the role as solely a supplier the company has a limited impact on
the decisions. However the relationship are static and well defined. The value capture is
stable and resources are used at a constant rate depending on the contract. The case
company has experience moving from supplier to partner after, a prove of concept and
knowledge phase.
