(chirumalla_enabling_2024)
DOI: 10.1016/j.techfore.2023.123044
1
Developed for ecosystem orchestrators as leaders or owner of 
platform and large resourceful  manufacturing companies. 
•
Ecosystem orchestrators are seen as the ones to set, develop 
(with partner) and impose certain standards on the ecosystem 
partners.
•
Ecosystem orchestrators bears the nurturing investment cost,  
facilitates investment and competence development of key 
ecosystem partners. 
•
Ecosystem orchestrators coordinate negotiation with e.g. risk-
benefits, give-take rules.  
Lack of distribution of 
roles and responsibilities 
of actors. 
Circular economy transformation of ecosystem require an 
orchestrator. 
The two-stage model of ecosystem transformation to 
circular economy run by ecosystem orchestrator.
1.
Readiness assessment – Goal: Identification of the 
transformation gap.
2.
Orchestration mechanisms (Standardization, 
negotiation, nurturing mechanism)
Bottom line outcome : Financial, environmental, social
Orchestrating industrial ecosystem in 
circular economy: A two-stage 
transformation model for large 
manufacturing companies. 
(parida_orchestrating_2019)
DOI: 10.1016/j.jbusres.2019.01.006
2
Focus on the commitment of supply chain players and the 
difference of vertical versus horizontal collaboration. 
Looking into the change of complexity with horizontal 
collaborations and the higher environmental gain. 
Facilitator can be Raw Material or Product Manufacture and 
Retailer.  
Lack of value co-creation, 
BM, and other CE 
strategies than recycling. 
Horizontal or vertical collaboration. 
Facilitator of supply chain collaboration. 
Looking into the different dynamics coming for different 
commitment in the SC:
•

•
Individual self-commitment
•
Service for collective commitment 
•
Service for individual commitment

Business model strategies approaches depending on 
collaboration intensity (open, semi-open or closed 
relationship).

Industry
2019
Parida et 
al., 2019
Orchestrating industrial ecosystem in 
circular economy: A two-stage 
transformation model for large 
manufacturing companies
10.1016/j.j
busres.20
19.01.006
Data collection involved 53 open-ended 
interviews with respondents from different 
organizational levels and units within the 
case companies. The interviews were 
conducted in two steps: first within the 
manufacturing company (ecosystem 
orchestrator) and then within the associated 
ecosystem partner's organization. 
Secondary data were also collected from 
archival materials and websites to validate 
results and enhance data triangulation.
Ecosystem orchestrators achieve the transition toward a 
circular economy in two stages: ecosystem readiness 
assessment and ecosystem transformation.
Collaboration transition thorugh two stages: Readiness 
assesment and Ecosystem transformation. The study 
identifies three key readiness assessments: External 
Environmental Assessment, Business Model Assessment, 
Ecosystem Partner Assessment. Ecosystem 
transformation involves three 
mechanisms:Standardization Mechanisms, Nurturing 
Mechanisms, Negotiation Mechanisms
The role of the ecosystem orchestrator is critical 
for implementing circular economy principles. 
Standardization, nurturing, and negotiation 
mechanisms are crucial for coordinating and 
managing ecosystem transformation.
Manufacturing 
2023
Havinga 
et al., 
2023
Unpacking ecosystem dynamics in the 
construction industry: The transition 
toward circular construction ecosystems
10.1016/j.j
clepro.202
3.137455
Data was collected through a multiple-case 
study involving seven ecosystems, with a 
combination of individual interviews and 
company website information. A total of 13 
semi-structured interviews were conducted 
across the seven case studies.
Standardization Mechanisms: Setting standards for 
ecosystem partners, Implementing modular product 
architecture, Resulted in higher control over design and greater 
predictability in materials and processes.Nurturing 
Mechanisms: Development of new routines and processes, 
Openness towards sharing core knowledge and intellectual 
property, Enhanced innovation and long-term relationships 
with suppliers, Ownership Mechanism: Greater ownership 
over the construction process in PDB approaches, Enabled 
higher standards and more significant sustainability practices. 
The PDB (Product Development Based) and PJB (Project-
Based) approaches are two distinct methodologies used in 
construction and project management, particularly regarding 
how partner alignment and interdependencies are managed.
Multi-case study suggests that product-based ecosystems 
have better alignment and orchestration mechanisms 
compared to project-based ecosystems.Construction 
ecosystems with PDB approaches have a higher degree of 
standardization and longer-term relationships.
The transition towards a circular economy in the 
construction industry is hindered by complex 
inter-organizational arrangements.

A triple helix framework for strategy

10.1016/j.j
clepro.202
2.132954
LR + Survey to rate the barriers collected 
between SC actors 
“understand the barriers of circular textile and clothing supply 
chain and to devise strategies to overcome the cause-group 
barriers.” ([Majumdar et al., 2022,) A triple helix framework 
focusing on textile industry, academia and government are 
suggested to overcom the barriers through collaboration, 
innovation and political support.
Lack of SC collaboration, effective planing and technical 
know-how are seen as the barriers with causial effect.  
“Industry-academia-government need to work 
collaboratively to create and develop circular clothing 
design, automated sorting technology, chemical recycling 
technology, collaboration with sorters and recyclers, 
economic competitiveness of recycled fibre, favouring 
circular textiles in public procurement, enacting incentives 
for circular practices and generating consumer 
awareness.” ([Majumdar et al., 2022, p. 11]
The most dominant barrier against CE transition  
is lack of technical know-how about circularity. 
“The strategies for circular textiles and clothing 
should start from the circular design focussing on 
longevity and recycling. Clothing having a single 
fibre is always easy to recycle. However, such 
clothing is functionally inferior. Circular design 
should facilitate easy sorting, disassembly and 
recycling. For example, most of the shirtsmade 
with organic cotton is often stitched with polyester 
sewing threads and therefore, their segregation at 
the post-consumer stage becomes difficult. In this 
context, collaboration between clothing 
designers, recyclers and design universities are 
needed so that designers can understand how to 
design for repair and recycling.” ([Majumdar et 
al., 2022, p. 9]) “New business models based on 
rental and subscription should be popularised 
against the traditional ownership-based model of 
clothing.” ([Majumdar et al., 2022, p. 9]

se.3040
The challenges to implement circular 
economy in manufacturing SCs identified 
from the literature, project and expert 
interviews
Dynamic capabilities (DC)  and CE challenges requiering 
organizations to constantly adapt to changing demands and 
develop resilience. Capabilites: sensing, seizing, and 
transforming of internal and external skills, resources and 
competences. Microfoundations are unique to specific 
organisations creating sustainable competition advantage.

Agarwal 
et al., 
2022
Framework development and evaluation of 
Industry 4.0 technological aspects 
towards improving the circular economy-
based supply chain
10.1108/IR
-10-2021-
0246
LR + industry interviews and voting
“The goal of the present study is to support sustainable 
practices by creating a decision management system in the SC 
network integrated with CE principles through Industry 4.0 
technologies.” Finding a connection between challenges in 
adapting CE and I 4.0 as a enabler. “These outcomes of the 
study might assist managers and policymakers in identifying 
the critical advantages of technological advancements in the 
context of CE implementation. This could assist in creating a 
more robust and efficient SC network.” 
“However, they are struggling to achieve the CE principles 
in their SC network due to various issues in its adaption. 
The present work aimed to provide a roadmap to the 
managers and policymakers of the industrial firm to 
establish CE principles in their practices.'""The highest 
weightage was attained by the criterion CT1, and the least 
weightage was attained by CT7, inferring that the 
information disruption in the SC network was the most 
dominant criteria hindering the CE adoption,” (Agarwal et 
al., 2022, p. 575). “the vulnerability in the decision 
management system stood at last”
“the findings of the present research imply that 
Industry 4.0 technologies might act as a 
breakthrough to overcome the difficulties 
occurred in the execution of CE in designing a 
manufacturing SC.” ([Agarwal et al., 2022, p. 575]
Manufacturing

Pharmaceutical industry, LR + 
semistructures interviews to shortlist the list

Collect barriers and enablers with focus on CSCM in pharma 
industry. The barriers are mainly towards adoption of CE 
strategies
Collaboration are one of the main barriers, because of 
lack of information sharing, complexity and competition 
on the market. 
Industrial symbiosis, reverse logsitic and block 
chain are the top ranked enabler for adoption of 
CE.

Overcoming barriers to cross-sector

Litterature study focusing on overcomming 
barriers for cross-sectorial supply chain 
collaboration. Mainly focus on India and 
with "experts" to range the barriers.“To 
answer the first research question, 27 
barriers to C-SC for CSM have been explored 
through a literature review;” ([Luthra et al., 
2022, p. 22]
“Collaborative value capture modelling and co-creating a 
change-making culture will enhance collaboration as this will 
create a shared value chain in which all collaborators mutually 
benefit. It will reduce issues of power asymmetry, mistrust and 
lack of a common vision. This strategy will also develop a 
change adapting model and facilitate legitimacy toward 
developing circular supply” ([Luthra et al., 2022, p. 19]. “There 
is a need for a C-SC enabled culture in organisations and co-
creation of production through waste as a resource. This 
includes formal joint decision making for enhancement of 
collaborative performance Brown et al. (2021).” ([Luthra et al., 
2022, p. 20](zotero://select/library/items/LPMCU4J6)) 
([pdf](zotero://open-
pdf/library/items/TCTHC4WB?page=20&annotation=KDCHVB
2U))
“The current study is based on the stakeholder theory’s 
notion suggesting that an organisation’s success is 
dependent on the relationship with its stakeholders. This 
implies that successful organisations consider the 
relationship with the stakeholders that they affect as well 
as those that affect them (Freeman, 1994). By adopting C-
SC, inter-relationships among stakeholders such as 
business, government and Non-Governmental 
Organisations (NGOs), are enhanced, amplifying the 
overall effectiveness of supply chains. The barriers 
identified in the study are assessed by their potential 
impact to limit stakeholder collaboration to develop CSCs. 
Stakeholder inter-relationships are a key element in 
developing C-SC and hence the barriers to it need to be 
evaluated.” ([Luthra et al., 2022, p. 3] This study assesses 
mutual trust and aligned interest from a collaboration 
perspective
Focus on indusrial waste, industrial symbiosis, 
zero waste. Resourced Based view (RBV) 
proposes the adoption of CE strategies are 
competences required to bring innovation in the

Barriers in implementation of CE models in 
practice - collected along VC thorugh 
interviews of different companies. 
Implementation of bottom-up data exchange practice in 
industry. Properly designed parameters could be used to 
codify relevant data without a negative impact on the 
confidentiality of companies. At the same time provice clarity 
of purpose for the CE. 
The collaboration and usage of 3-party data is still not 
utalized. Data collected and needed in different parts of 
the VC are not transferd to the one needing it. Data are 
equalized to know-how, strategy, valueable ressource, 
competition advantage and not to be shared with others. 
Parameterization could facilitate collaboration among the 
different actors on a common goal/target for optimization 
without the need of sharing "to much" information. 
“For instance, data can promote circularity by 
providing the necessary input to determine 
circularity parameters (” (Serna-Guerrero et al., 
2022, p. 2). A table of overview of different types 
of data to facilitate different CE R-strategies.

LR
Focus on managers role for implementation of CE. Managers 
should guid the transition, plan with barriers and 
strategies/possible solution for these barriers making it 
possible to perpare transition at the lowest cost and maximize 
results. The managers need to have fully knowledge of CE 
principle. They should collaborate on strategic level and in the 
formation of CSC implement I 4.0 to build sustainable SC.
Collaboration in SC can positively impact in two ways; 
formal thorugh contracts and standards and informal 
thorugh trust and cooperation between the actors in the 
chain. The main barrier is the lack of collaboration 
between the actors in the SC. 
For organizations aiming to transition from linear 
economy to CE must be aware of their products 
and process must undergo af significant changes. 
Informations tech I4.0 can contribute to the 
formation of SC. Also the importance in changing 
the culture within the organization,

Wielopols
ki and 
Bulthuis, 
2023
The Better Building Initiative — a 
Collaborative Ecosystem Involving All 
Stakeholders as Catalyst to Accelerate the 
Adoption of Circular Economy Innovations 
in the Construction Sector
10.1007/s
43615-
022-
00205-6
LR
“CE approach to innovation can be practically implemented in 
the building and construction industry to provide an 
ecosystem for innovation, which is based on a manifesto that 
fosters flexible, transparent, and exponentially scalable CE 
innovation targets. Thereby, we propose a globally open 
platform approach to catalyze effective collaboration between 
intrinsically motivated stakeholders in the absence of external 
or regulatory pressure and consequently accelerate the 
innovation and implementation dynamics. The term “catalyst” 
is being used deliberately to make the bridge to a chemical 
catalysis process, in which the activation energy for a 
substantial transformation is lowered, and like this, the 
transformation process is exponentially accelerated (Fig. 1). 
Such a shift from incremental to exponential change dynamics 
requires an innovation environment that is interdisciplinary 
(involving all stakeholders) and resolves the current blockers, 
which stem from the building industry structure/culture 
(Fig. 2).” ([Wielopolski and Bulthuis, 2023, p. 727]
CE is a systemic approach requiring the involvment of 
interdiceplinary stakeholders to become effective. 
Adopting CE approach has the potential to accelerate 
development and adpotion of innovation in a sector 
because of the needed multidisciplinary collaboration 
To ensure true and ambitius innovation in the 
construction sector, retaining ownership, 
motivate lifetime extention of the material is 
suggessted. CE is currently considered to rovide 
practical solutions that can accelerate holistic 
innovation making the construction industry more 
sustainable. Circular innovation requireing not 
only change of material or process but also 
business model transformation. 
Construction 
industry

LR
Framework of drivers, practices and barriers for 
implementation of CE in SC. Insight into the best way to get CE 
implemented in an enterprise's SC from different stakeholder 
point of view.
Barriers, drivers and practices for organization and SC. 
Barriers calsified after internal / external and by how one 
or more stakeholders are related to the barriers. Only the 
ones containing more than one stakeholder are collected. 
CE practices: how CE should be adapted into a 
SC. Main practice is to design a product for 
remanufacturing, make sufficient laws and 
policies so enterprices are willing to adopt the CE 
in SC.

 and

“The failure in certain standards for collecting and recycling of

Lack of governmental knowlegde and regulation to promote 
CE . “policy makers should focus on preparing new rules and 
regulations as well as revising and modifying the current ones 
to be in line with the transition to the CE. The companies 
conducting the circular activities should be certified by the 
related authorities to prevent the misuse of circular activities 
and to regulate the market. In addition to that, the standards 
should be set in order to assure a uniform level of quality 
which is critical to achieve the desired circular and 
sustainable outcome.”

 Kazancoglu 
et al., 2020
 Integration and 
collaboration: 
Integration and collaboration: Lack of 
sharing information and communication
Lack of effective communication, integration, and cooperation 
among SC members which will increase their competitiveness 
and productivity.
Long-term relationships of the SC is based on transparency 
and information sharing. The length of SC and the nature of 
competition they are reluctant to share information among 
firms. “The trust is the key factor for convincing the textile 
supply to implement such a new business model. Hence, 
information sharing and improved communication should be 
the initial step. It is going to contribute the traceability of the 
products and services. Block chain-based technology and 
radio frequency identification (RFID) can be used in 
traceability for textile supply chain (Kelepouris, Pramatari, & 
Doukidis, 2007; Rusinek, Zhang, & Radziwill, 2018). 
Afterwards, the transparency of circular operations in all 
stages of the SC will be achieved. Finally, this will proceed 
with the SC to achieve a common vision and shared goals” 
(Kazancoglu et al., 2020, p. 1487)

problems from raw material to finished product. Changing 
suppliers often prevent achieving the CE goals.

 Integration and collaboration: Lack of

partnership throughout the SC. This may be due to a lack of 
common vision, fear of losing control, or lack of trust between 
them.
Long-term relationships of the SC is based on transparency 
and information sharing. The length of SC and the nature of 
competition they are reluctant to share information among 
firms. Trust, transparency, transition of linear operation in SC, 
information sharing, allingment of vision

18
Exploratory 
research method
Textile
recycling 
(C2C)
 Kazancoglu 
et al., 2020
Cost 
High investment cost
Companies should change their infrastructure in transition to the 
CE. Use of new technology, certification process, and training 
employees increase the investment cost. In addition, the cost of 
recycled fibers, used in the production and the collection of 
waste fabrics, are high.
Therefore, firms do not want to invest and take risks because 
of the uncertainty in profitability and the long-term benefits it 
will provide within the SC. “These high costs are related to the 
producing and/or selling circular products. In order for textile 
companies to benefit from economies of scale, they need to 
increase the amount of products they produce. This increase 
in production quantity depends on consumer demand for 
circular products. Therefore, the more information 
consumers have about these products and its benefits, the 
more they will buy these products, then the more producers 
will increase production quantities and eventually, unit cost 
per product will decrease.” ([Kazancoglu et al., 2020, p. 
1487]
19
Exploratory 
research method
Textile
recycling 
(C2C)
 Kazancoglu 
et al., 2020
Cost 
Uncertainty in profitability
The uncertainty in profitability and return on investment affect 
companies’ expectations and causes them to be reluctant in 
making investments.
Therefore, firms do not want to invest and take risks because 
of the uncertainty in profitability and the long-term benefits it 
will provide within the SC

 Kazancoglu 
et al., 2020
Cost 
Failure to provide the scale of production
Since the demand for circular products in textile industry is low, 
there is currently no large volume of production, which prevents 
companies to benefit from the advantages of economies of 
scale.
Therefore, firms do not want to invest and take risks because 
of the uncertainty in profitability and the long-term benefits it 
will provide within the SC. “These high costs are related to the 
producing and/or selling circular products. In order for textile 
companies to benefit from economies of scale, they need to 
increase the amount of products they produce. This increase 
in production quantity depends on consumer demand for 
circular products. Therefore, the more information 
consumers have about these products and its benefits, the 
more they will buy these products, then the more producers 
will increase production quantities and eventually, unit cost 
per product will decrease.” ([Kazancoglu et al., 2020, p. 
1487]

separating recyclable products to turn them into high quality 
raw materials” ([Kazancoglu et al., 2020, p. 1486]

Technical barriers
Lack of information systems and data 
management
while in the linear economy, the number of stakeholders is not 
high and relationships among them are usually one sided, in a 
CSC the complexity increases tremendously resulting in a greater 
need for data management skills (Ozkan-Ozen et al., 2020). 
Indeed, a lack of information systems and data management is 
considered a relevant barrier. The security and the capacityof 
data storage systems are also denounced as important issues.

Data availability and validity
Lack of data reliability and verification because of self-
declaration and no standards . Lack of data availability.

data.”–Delphi comment. “We often have to rely on the 
self–declaration of many upstream partners. There's no real 
verification mechanism in place for many sustainability 
dimensions.”–OEMCo2 “Of course, the miners will vouch for 
absolutely no child labor in their operations. But before we 
checked every single mine, how can we be 
sure?”–RefiningCo. “When we recollect the battery, much of 
the data we get out of the battery management system is 
insufficient, inaccessible, or 
unstandardized.”–BatteryComponentCo

Information gathering

Transparency: Data sharing and balance 
appropirate disclosure both ways
Lack of transparency both ways between upstream and 
downstream partners. OEM/downstream requireing transpareny 
and data but does not deliver them self. Difficulty in balancing 
appropriate disclosure levels with exchange partners
Data sharing on sustainability should go both ways. Currently, 
it's imbalanced. It's more the OEMs and other downstream 
players requesting transparency. The upstream should also 
see the downstream data, this would increase the willingness 
to share.”–Mining Co2 “Organizations might opt out at a

shouldn't be a one-way street.”–Delphi comment “We often 
deal with bulk traders for our input materials like refined 
cobalt or nickel. Those partners are rather tight-lipped 
regarding transparency about the exact product origin. It's not 
in their interest to share this, and they are not forced to do 
so.”–ActiveMaterialsCo2. “Particularly upstream 
stakeholders pointed out that transparency often only goes 
one way when powerful OEMs request data but are unwilling 
to share themselves”

Information gathering

Incentivization: Data gathering / 
Transparency
Lack of incentivization for data gathering. Difficulty to balance 
incentives for information sharing with potential of penalization 
for non-compliance.
Data access must be steered between the car or battery 
manufacturer and the entities who want the data, for 
example, the consumers. It has to be a permissioned access 
and this complex governance has to be worked out. There has 
to be an agreed framework for this.”PolicyMaker3. “It all 
comes down to commercial contracts and incentives. 
Whenever they see value in transmitting that data down the 
supply, they will see to its execution.”–RecyclingCo1. “It is 
essential that disclosure is first stimulated in the battery 
value chain before we focus on penalizing. If a trader 
discloses all mining locations with a clear intention to 
increase sustainability, that should be positive and should 
not lead to a business decline.”PolicyMaker1. The 
assessment has to be a solution-oriented benchmarking that 
acknowledges progress rather than blaming actors. That is 
what's often happening right now and prevents actors from 
becoming more transparent.”–RefiningCo.

Information gathering

Regulation
Restrictive antitrust regulations. Risk of overregulating 
sustainability assessment for batteries. 
The assessment has to be a solution-oriented benchmarking

is what's often happening right now and prevents actors from

stakeholders, to increase the 
flexibility.”BatteryComponentCo. “Particularly in Europe, 
data protection and competition laws are sometimes 
incompatible with creating full transparency on certain 
sustainability issues. At least everybody is cautious not to 
breach any of this, which makes holistic sustainability 
assessments more cumbersome.”OEMCo1. “Centralized 
databases for assessment along the value chain might be a 
big issue, as this might be a breach in antitrust.”–Delphi 
comment

Information gathering

Standards: Customization and variability of 
standards
Lack of customization of sustainability standards. Value chain 
step specific standards. High variability of sustainability 
standards.
“Standards are not specific enough for certain value chain 
steps (e.g., OEMs send sustainability questionnaires to 
miners, which are actually defined to evaluate refiners). It's a 
little bit like putting a circle in a square.”–Delphi comment. 
“We should have a fair bit of flexibility for which kind of 
standards will be accepted; the mining site should ideally be 
able to choose a credible reporting standard, out of the many 
that exist, that fits their operations.”–MiningCo2. “A myriad 
of national standards exist across the battery value chain, 
many of them with completely different concepts. [...] 
Stakeholders will be very hesitant to get certified by yet 
another standard. We face this problem often when we try to 
onboard new suppliers.”OEMCo1

Information gathering 
and sharing barriers
Competition: Market information
Fear of losing market opportunities through information sharing. 
Lack of willingness to share due to confidentiality requirements
“Where companies like cathode producers actually get their 
recycling feed is a big competitive advantage [...]. Where do 
they get the recycled material from is something they will not 
easily like to share.”–RecyclingCo1. “To give an example of 
battery recyclers versus battery manufacturers: Full 
transparency on the value of materials in the battery that can 
be recycled will enable upfront calculation of process cost 
minus resale value. The battery recycler will accept only 
valuable batteries, that leave them with net positive value 
waste, and leave the manufacturer with a decreasing 
competitive advantage through this former proprietary 
information.”–BatteryProducerCo.  “The industry, or we as a 
battery manufacturer, will never agree to give to a third party 
the [bill of materials] of our batteries. You might sometimes 
not even want to share this across 
plants.”BatteryProducerCo. “Confidentiality will always be 
an issue, no matter what, companies will always be hesitant 
to share data with direct or indirect competitors. This is 
always a core requirement when we supply battery 
management systems.”–BatteryComponentProducer

Financial/economic 
barriers:
Economic attractiveness
Uncertainty regarding economic attractiveness of sustainable 
supply chain practices
“There's a big question mark on many environmental 
practices. Raw materials are much cheaper than recycled 
materials, recycling processes are not profitable, there's high 
uncertainty about the value of second-life 
applications.”–BatteryComponentCo “We are already 
pushed to produce batteries as cheaply as possible. If 
sustainable criteria become fully transparent and we are 
measured against that, we will have to increase our prices to 
look good on that front.”–BatteryProducerCo. “industry 
representatives stated that more sustainable practices such 
as using recycled instead of raw materials are not necessarily 
always economically more attractive. As cost-efficiency or 
profitability remain among the most prominent performance 
indicators business organizations are measured against, 
more transparency on sustainable practices is not always 
aligned with their interest.” ([Gebhardt et al., 2022, p. 214]

60
LR+experts(9) + 
Case study (IS)
IS / 
Manufact
uring 
Chari et al., 
2022
Multi-domain 
challenges
Complexity and uncertainty in material flow, 
BM, product design, Service and distribution
“A practical definition of CE principles applicable to 
manufacturing is urgently required to remedy this lack of clarity. 
This definition extends to the SC as well: if business models are 
not aligned across the SC, then circular solutions may not be 
technically or economically feasible, for example, products are 
not designed for longevity, repair, disassembly and 
remanufacturing, or cannot be collected and reprocessed in 
ways that retain their value. In addition, circular strategies, 
definitions and practices are not standardized, complicating 
conceptualization in the sense stage.” ([Chari et al., 2022, p. 
2507]
“The type of company information, data or knowledge will 
affect the allocation of responsibilities between suppliers and 
users (causal relationship). Dynamic collaboration in the 
value network and the type of competencies that exist 
outside of the focal company are other aspects to consider 
for successful collaboration within the SC.” (Chari et al., 
2022, p. 2510) “Companies will constantly need to iterate 
between measuring (collect data and select indicators) and 
improving (interpret results) due to constantly changing 
business environments when manufacturing processes and 
business models are developed, modified or changed.” 
(Chari et al., 2022, p. 2510)

Lack of capabilities
Mismatch of capacities/capabilites
“A practical definition of CE principles applicable to 
manufacturing is urgently required to remedy this lack of clarity. 
This definition extends to the SC as well: if business models are 
not aligned across the SC, then circular solutions may not be 
technically or economically feasible, for example, products are 
not designed for longevity, repair, disassembly and 
remanufacturing, or cannot be collected and reprocessed in 
ways that retain their value. In addition, circular strategies, 
definitions and practices are not standardized, complicating 
conceptualization in the sense stage.” (Chari et al., 2022, p. 
2507)
“As Porter and Linde (1995) described, manufacturing 
companies trade capabilities against each other to prioritise 
certain competitive capabilities. However, others suggest 
that three to five capabilities achieved simultaneously could 
give rise to long-term improvements and competitive 
advantage (Ünal & Shao, 2019).” (Chari et al., 2022, p. 2510). 
“Competitive capabilities such as cost, delivery, quality and 
flexibility can help firms achieve long-term sustainable 
competitive goals in SCs (Ünal & Shao, 2019).” ([Chari et al., 
2022, p. 2511] “IS transition is generally hindered in the 
implementation phase. That is, the concept of selling waste 
as an input to another industry is easily conceptualised, but 
the more challenging step is in the implementation or the 
‘seizing’ capabilities phase. A quantity mismatch of by-
products can hinder the seizing of symbiotic opportunities 
and cannot be overlooked in this phase.” ([Chari et al., 2022, 
p. 2507]

Standards
No standardized conceptualization of CE 
No standardized conceptualization of CE among organizations is 
a barrier to implement CE practices. 
“Similarly, data should be able to be measured and defined 
consistently for successful cross-functional collaboration 
among partners. The plurality in CE definitions and 
interpretations makes it difficult to build new business 
models and tools (value capture) within the organisation and 
in other SC partners” (Chari et al., 2022, p. 2510).

Standards
Measurement 
Lack of knowledge in using the appropirate quantitative methods 
to measure the environmental impact 
“Similarly, data should be able to be measured and defined 
consistently for successful cross-functional collaboration 
among partners. The plurality in CE definitions and 
interpretations makes it difficult to build new business 
models and tools (value capture) within the organisation and 
in other SC partners” (Chari et al., 2022, p. 2510). “For 
instance, expert B mentioned that some metal companies 
communicate the weight of waste produced, others express 
waste as the number of products being disposed of, and 
others define waste in terms of monetary value. These 
different metrics and indicators of waste make it difficult to 
communicate between companies, but also miss the value 
embedded in waste” ([Chari et al., 2022, p. 2507]

Chari et al., 
2022
Trade-off in I4.0
Using new technology giving sustainablility trade-off needed to 
be taken into acount
“from the interviews that sustainability trade-offs exist in 
firms when using I4.0 technologies. For instance, although 
blockchain is being used to monitor material flows and trace 
products along the SCs, one needs to be aware of the energy 
source of these technologies.” (Chari et al., 2022, p. 2505). 
automotive industries are developing new materials using I4.0 
technologies to build structural components of electric 
vehicles. Using these new materials could present a 
sustainability issue even though driving the electric vehicle 
has a positive environmental impact. “The experts stated that 
novel digital manufacturing technologies like 3D printing are 
being used to develop new materials, optimize productivity 
and create profits; without considering the quality, content, 
behaviour and lifetime of these materials.” ([Chari et al., 
2022, p. 2509]. “Workforce skills and knowledge such as 
programming, big data analytics and artificial intelligence are 
important tacit capabilities (Bag, Gupta, & Kumar, 2021). I4.0 
can support procurement for resource flexibility (adaptive 
capability), integrate internal and external integration 
(absorptive capability) and innovation (innovative capability) 
to gain competitive advantage (Bag et al., 2020). Conversely, 
greater the organisational flexibility, higher the I4.0 
technology adoption (Bag, Gupta, & Kumar, 2021).” ([Chari et 
al., 2022, p. 2512)
84
LR+experts(9) + 
Case study (IS)
IS / 
Manufact
uring 
I4.0 for CE
Chari et al., 
2022
Lack of understanding, commitment and  
Organizational willingness
Lack of understanding and commitment for transforming 
business-as-usual activities. Organisation has to develop 
strategic and capabilities to integrate, prevent errors and 
increase efficiency. 
Despite the potential of I4.0 for CE, decision makers may not 
recognise it as a viable strategy. Expert F pointed to a lack of 
understanding and commitment, without which I4.0 will not 
help change business-as-usual activities. This showcases a 
strong link between organisational and digital technological 
capabilities. Some organisations may already have digital 
infrastructure in place (robots, sensors, IT capabilities) but 
have yet to develop strategic and organisational capabilities 
to integrate these and make them more flexible to prevent 
errors and increase efficiency.

Chari et al., 
2022
Lack of trust, data and transparency
“The empirical data pointed to trust, data and transparency 
issues; thus, leadership and strategy capabilities are needed 
for I4.0 to support collaboration capabilities which play a 
significant role for CE transition.” ([Chari et al., 2022, p. 
2512]. “Trust is an important factor, as it is needed to 
overcome the problems associated with moving by-products 
or waste, such as variations in quality, cost and time. Trust 
can be achieved by the development of skills, which can 
improve collaborations and communication between the 
different entities of the SC. Accessing reliable information is 
critical for larger commodity supply chains, where a single 
source of information (supplier) may be insufficient to 
guarantee trust (Villena & Gioia, 2018). Specifically, lower-tier 
suppliers have typically less awareness and knowledge about 
sustainability-related practices, receive less pressure from 
public society and are located in countries where social and 
environmental regulations are not prioritized (Villena & Gioia, 
2018).” ([Chari et al., 2022, p. 2507]

Uncertainties
Uncertainties: Quality, Availability and 
delivery of secondary resources. Uncertain 
demand for CE products and service 
High variablility in the quality, volume and delivery of customer 
returns in take-back systems. Contractual agreements and 
supply market. 
In this regard, the expert panelists shared that secondary 
resources (e.g., recycled MCPs) pose quality, availability, and 
delivery uncertainties that cause delays and disruptions in 
CSCs.“Moreover, the expert panelists stressed consumers’ 
poor opinions about the quality and performance of 
recovered products. Finally, they affirmed that take-back 
systems pose risks regarding the quality, volume, and delivery 
of customer returns, as CSCs face high variability of returns, 
stocks, and lead times.” ([De Lima and Seuring, 2023, p. 8]

Organizational 
uncertainty 
Organizational resistivity
Top executives locked into the business-as-usual model. Lack of 
flexibility
“Regarding “resistance to change by top management, ” the 
expert panelists argued that a linear mindset hinders the 
transition to the CE. If top executives are “locked into” the 
business-as-usual model, they are likely to delay their 
companies’ implementation of the CE. This behavior is known 
as organizational resistivity, primarily influenced by fears of 
financial risks or environmental factors such as cultural and 
market structures.” (De Lima and Seuring, 2023, p. 
8).“According to the expert panelists, the transition toward 
the CE requiresthat companies adopt flexible plans and 
strategies. Attention should therefore be given to CE projects 
and activities that are more likely to enhance cost-efficiency 
and foster win-win opportunities.” ([De Lima and Seuring, 
2023, p. 10]

Reluctance of acceptance of CE 
Pressens of companies doing linear business, stakeholders in 
network not willing to adopt the new busienss model. One of the 
main barriers against transitioning. 
“Kirchherr et al. (2018), as stated in the study, all companies 
in the SC should adopt the CE model in the formation of the 
CSC. As Tura et al. (2019) point out the fact that some of the 
stakeholders within the network are not willing to adopt the 
new business model and that the corporate culture does not 
support the companies in this direction is considered as an 
important barrier. Therefore, adopting the linear economy 
model is an important barrier for this type of company.” 
(Kazancoglu et al., 2022, p. 538)

Lack of accademical and feasible 
information about CE principle
Seen as the most influencing factor in the cause of transitionning 
problems. 
“ack of academic and feasible information about the CE 
principles is a barrier to the CE. The lack of information 
regarding implementing CE, especially about the benefits of 
the CE for companies, is the biggest barrier for companies to 
invest and implement the CE (Herrero Rodríguez 2017). As 
stated in the study of Pasqualotto (2015), companies do not 
have sufficient technical information, knowledge, and 
managerial capacity for companies to change their current 
business models and implement the CE. In addition to the 
lack of information, education programme is a critical barrier 
in the implementation of sustainable production (Malek and 
Desai 2019). Likewise, Rizos et al. (2015) and Tura et al. 
(2019) emphasised that the lack of technical and managerial 
knowledge and skills is an important barrier especially in the 
adoption of the CE business model of SMEs.” ([Kazancoglu et 
al., 2022, p. 539]

Lack of traceability and data
applying CE is found risky and difficult to monitor.
“Beyond performance assessment problems, companies 
may hesitate to accept the CE as a business model and this 
affects yarn, fabric and garment manufacturers, designers, 
brand owners and retailers, and governments/policymakers 
(Cao et al. 2008; Govindan and Hasanagic 2018; Kirchherr et 
al. 2018). This happens when there are negative reactions to 
change, short-term focus and applying the CE is found risky 
(Kumar et al. 2019; Kumar and Suganya 2019). Problems of 
tracking and tracing refer difficulty of monitoring the 
product’s life cycle and material flows in the CE experienced 
by yarn, fabric and garment manufacturers, brand owners 
and retailers, collectors and recyclers (Elia, Grazia Gnoni, and 
Tornese 2017; Govindan and Hasanagic 2018; Kumar and 
Suganya 2019). This difficulty also occurs because of the lack 
of data (e.g. about material location, the determination of 
components and materials and their related typology, status, 
and quantity) and suitable information for decision-making 
processes” ([Kazancoglu et al., 2022, p. 527]

Lack of incentives for circularity in SC
Lack of incentives for circularity in SC is thorugh analysis 
significantly influencing lack of SC collaboration. The incentives 
of CE are to low.
“Lack of incentives for circularity in supply chains (B14) is 
signifi cantly influencing lack of supply chain collaboration 
(B12), lack of effective planning for CSC (B15), lack of 
circular performance monitoring and evaluation (B16) and 
lack of benchmarking for circularity standards (B17).” 
(Majumdar et al., 2022, p. 8) “The textile and clothing supply 
chain is very price sensitive and most of the suppliers 
operating in South Asian countries carry out the lean part of 
production for their foreign buyers who leverage on the low 
labour cost in these countries. Therefore, though brands can 
attract the consumers showing their commitment to 
circularity, the financial incentive does not percolate back in 
the upstream side of the clothing supply chain.” (Majumdar 
et al., 2022, p. 8)

Serna-
Guerrero et 
al., 2022
Information 
exchange 
Lack of natural ways to build trust between 
companies when not having a customer-
supplier dynamics
Trust as a fundamental part of the ecosystem. 
“This amplifies the need for trust among the actors in the 
value chain (Rajala et al., 2018). While trust is strongly driven 
by social aspects and people, it has been noted that the 
strategies for CE and circular manufacturing tend to ignore 
them as fundamental parts of the ecosystem (Acerbi and 
Taisch, 2020).” (Serna-Guerrero et al., 2022, p. 2).  “The 
inherent challenge is that there is no natural way to build trust 
for companies that do not otherwise conduct business with 
each other. On the other hand, customer-supplier dynamics 
are capable of building relationships of trust, hence 
facilitating data exchange. One of our interviewees (Process 
engineer, Manufacturing) stated for example: “with 
customers, we share quite a lot of data because, [if] we get to 
develop our products together with the customer, [it] brings 
value to both.” Perhaps it is necessary to promote a vision in 
which, due to the cyclic nature of the circular economy, all 
actors in the value chain are at some point customers and 
suppliers. As mentioned in the work by Gupta et al. (2019) 
trust, commitment, and accountability are prerequisites for 
information exchange, an aspect vital to implementation of 
circular supply chains.” ([Serna-Guerrero et al., 2022, p. 5]

Information 
exchange 
Data is equated with valuable know-how
Confidentiality practices hinders openness 
“And according to Barbrook (1998), companies get the full 
value of their data most likely by making it available for 
others, enabling so-called “hi-tech gift economy.” As 
mentioned in the introduction, it is data transfer and not its 
collection that determines the most efficient systems. 
Findings supporting this claim have also been made by other 
researchers. For instance, Turunen et al. (2018) found that in 
industrial service networks, mere data ownership or exclusive 
access do not provide strategic advantages. Instead, they 
suggest companies succeed when they can utilize and steer 
vast information flows.” ([Serna-Guerrero et al., 2022, p. 
6](zotero://select/library/items/ECAYRMNJ)) 
([pdf](zotero://open-
pdf/library/items/BHE3NLLF?page=6&annotation=GZ9ANJJC)
)

Serna-

Information 
exchange 
Data discrepancies hinder utilization of data 
from other actors. 
Different standards, systems, formats and structure makes it 
labor, time and financial expensive to share data. There is a 
practical gap in standardizing data. 
“Each company within the value chain currently has its own 
data systems, formats, and structures, leading to data not 
being commensurate across the value chain. As stated by 
one of our interviewees (Technology Director, Applications): 
“If we want [data] from some customer’s background system 
or from some other device manufacturer’s system ... there 
are many, many types of standards and the data [are] in 
different formats and there are different information systems, 
data sources, and it is quitesome mishmash (sic) in the end. 
So, if that data [is transferred] to our servers ... then it might 
be that it needs nine months of software development before 
one gets computers to send [the data].” Utilizing combined 
data from several sources would require transforming it into a 
standardized format, which was considered laborious, time-
consuming and costly by the interviewees.” ([Serna-Guerrero 
et al., 2022, p. 6]

Lack of data sharing structures
Companies lack protocol to transform data collected by other

“Data discrepancies also indicate the absence of data-
sharing structures throughout the value chain. While 
companies did claim to have sufficient resources and 
capabilities for collecting and utilizing their own data, these 
capabilities do not yet exist at a system-level perspective. 
Companies do not have protocols to successfully transform 
data” (Serna-Guerrero et al., 2022, p. 6)

Serna-
Guerrero et

Information 
exchange 
Lack of quantitative and qualitative 
parameters to evaluate circularity
Parameter so collective actions would focous on one 
level/target. Foster cooperation for the optimization of the CE. 
Discussions at system level would be carried out based on their 
impact on the parameter value at any given point in the life cycle, 
"black box" internal operation from the other actors. 
“Utilizing circularity indicators requires vast data inputs, but 
they can provide clear guidance on which information is 
relevant to trace the impact of circularity strategies. Indeed, 
parametrization is a prerequisite for the effective cooperation 
and promotion of CE ecosystems, including management 
decisions, policies, and new technologies. Circularity 
indicators may also serve as a practical vehicle by which 
relevant data is exchanged without compromising sensitive 
data of companies. In the absence of system-wide indicators, 
companies are at risk of making uninformed decisions related 
to circularity without fully understanding the impacts and 
ramifications of their actions.” (Serna-Guerrero et al., 2022, 
p. 8)

tion 
industry 
Wielopolski 
and Bulthuis, 
2023
Incremental 
innovation 
Narrowing circularity goals to avoid multiple 
stakeholder complexity 
Often bilateral collaboration with the closest or most trusted 
partners in the value chain. Risk-averse and slow to adopt, 
implement and scale innovation 
far-reaching, scalable circular innovation need broad scope 
of innovation by supporting all ecosystem 
actors/stakeholders in collaborating with them 
simultaneously. Suggesting innovation environment to allign 
all stakeholders from key parts of the value chain. Circular 
innovation requireing not only change of material or process 
but also business model transformation

 LR + 
semistructures 
interviews to 
shortlist the list of 
barriers
Pharma 
industry
Khan and Ali, 
2022
Lack of inofrmation sharing, cooperation 
and collaboration  among pharmaceutical 
industries
Lack of cooperation among the entire supply chain network 
including manufacturers, wholesalers, GPs, pharmacies, and 
patients
“Hence, the challenges faced by the pharmaceutical 
companies in adopting the CSCM such as the lack of financial 
resources, market challenges, and lack of collaboration 
within the supply chain network can be surmounted by the 
adoption of strategies such as industrial symbiosis, RL 
infrastructure, and block chain technology accordingly. These 
strategies or enablers would not just ensure environmental 
sustainability but would also ensure economic sustainability. 
The aforementioned enabler would not only give a 
competitive advantage to the companies but would also 
create employment opportunities. Thus through this way, 
several targets of SDGs can be achieved.” ([Khan and Ali, 
2022, p. 13720]

C1
A shortage of long-term resources hinders effective orchestration and partnership 
success.
”Sometimes we take the lead, and other times we end up going
with it.”... ”And other times, we might find ourselves in a situation where we opt 
out
of something because we don’t have the resources to take on a leading role. And
if no one else has the resources to take the lead either, I do worry that some things
might fall in between chairs. It’s a huge challenge to overcome and orchestrate
everything.” (C1 Q.15.) ”I would say it requires strength—it takes resources to 
make it happen.
Going back to resources, a short-term effort of 14 days with some external support
is not enough. You need sustained resources and competencies to invest in it. And
that, in itself, can be a challenge. If a company doesn’t have a financial surplus but
could significantly benefit from collaborating with others, they might never get 
there
simply because they lack the opportunity to do so.”...”So, there could be support

Scaling the solution for long-term implementation in the value chain remains a 
challenge.
”That’s the challenge we’re facing—how do you scale? It’s not an art to
run a small pilot project in one or two cities. The real challenge is scaling it into
something with a significant impact—something that becomes an integrated part 
of
1
C2
Challenge of standardizing the scaling phase and develop a operating model.
”We are also in a scaling phase. How do we standardize this? It’s one thing to do
it in Denmark, but how can we scale this further? You are actually showing many
of the key elements needed for a systemic approach to scaling, and that’s exactly
what we are working on now. How do we develop what we call an operating model.”
(C2. Q 31)
1
B
Lack of economy of scale to collaborate with suppliers. 
More challenging to collect data because lack of scale and placement of SC geo-
graphically. Low scale production. Lacking economy of scale to collaborate with
manufactures in SC. The manufactures in the SC are bigger than the company so
they are a small fish. We get custom-made items in relatively small volumes. If we 
go out and tell some-
one that we want it in a specific way, it usually costs a bit extra. And I think we’re
missing some economy of scale to be able to change much.”... ”That’s just natural.
And as I see it, we simply don’t have a large enough critical mass. Our suppliers
are big enough that they have other, larger customers than us.” (B Q. 9.)
1
C1, C2
Uncertainty around confidentiality creates a perceived barrier, with no clear solutions 
to prevent it from impacting the process

competitor? How do you share information in a way that doesn’t harm your own 
production?”... ”But how do you prevent it from being perceived as a barrier and 
instead create a process where everyone can fully engage and contribute in the 
best possible way to achieve the highest level of circularity—or whatever the goal 
may be?”(C1 Q. 21) ”Of course, when you first enter into a collaboration with direct 
competitors and so on, there is a natural caution about how things will unfold.”(C2. 
Q. 12, 13)

C1 
Orchestrating partnerships demands long-term resource allocation.
”Also because it requires a continuous resource commitment from the companies 
involved in a partnership. It’s not something you just do for three months and then
it’s done. It requires the company to allocate resources over a long period of time. 
So, it might make sense to outsource it—just like you would hire an accountant.” 
(C1 Q. 13)

not even at the company level. It could very well be a service that companies could

C2
Difficult to find recycling or value recovery partner with viable business case. 
ECOSYSTEM: ”Getting a recycling partner up and running in this field, which is
a relatively new area, has also been a challenge. Again, when it comes to value
recovery companies—how many are there, and can they build a viable business
case around this? That’s the reality of business—if there isn’t a strong business
case, if they don’t see a financial opportunity or a strategic investment, it won’t be
sustainable in the long run. That’s why it has been crucial for us to establish a close
and strategic partnership.” (C2. Q. 14)

Lack of standardization of products or material types in the industry

than they were initially—it would be a step in the right direction. Standardization 
can
help remove some roadblocks, and I believe that’s the way forward.” (C1 Q. 28)

K
Phase 5: Interview Guide
The structure of the Interviews is described below. The interview was separated into 3
parts.
1. Part. Setting the scene by describing the objective, motivation, and goal of the
master’s thesis and barrier collection.
2. Part. Ask the following questions from this interview guide while adding follow-up
questions based on the conversation. The goal is to understand the companies’
current situations, collaboration, and experienced barriers without priming them with
material found in the literature.
3. Part. Showing an overview of themes from the barriers collection to collect additional
examples or context on barriers.
Part 2. Questions
The aim of the interview is to hold it as a conversation, where there is room to dive into
elements said by the interviewee. There are overall 3 areas of focus distributed on 6
Questions. If the company is coming into the subject of a question through one of the
others it is not used.
The interview aims to be a conversation, allowing space to dive into topics introduced
by the interviewee. There are three focus areas, distributed across six questions. If a
particular question-answer naturally emerges in response to another question, the corre-
sponding question is not asked.
Initial experience
The overall rationale is to assess the interviewee’s and the company’s knowledge and
experience in Circular Economy.
• Q. What is your role in the company?
Rationale: Title, Experience, Level in Organization
• Q. How has the company implemented a circular economy strategy?
Rationale: CE strategies, Level of readiness/implementation
Collaboration and Relationship
The overall rationale is to determine their experience with collaboration and bring forward
this experience when asking about barriers in focus 3. Also to understand where they
collaborate their development and intentions.
• Q. Do you work in collaboration or partnership with any company? About what?
Rationale: About what, intention, experience
• Q. How does it work?
Rationale: Roles, dynamics
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
153
==PAGE:170==
Barriers and resources
The overall rationale is to collect barriers or resource-heavy challenges from their experi-
ence with collaboration. Also to understand how they have overcome them.
• Q: Did you have any challenges on which you have used a lot of resources (time,
knowledge, test)?
Rationale: What was the challenge, was it solved, how was it solved
• Q: Did you have any challenges that stopped the collaboration or development of
innovation together?
Rationale: What was the challenge, why did it stop the collaboration, how far was
the collaboration in development
Part 3. Overview of themes from barrier collection
This overview was used to collect additional challenges. The time of introduction of it
depended on the context of the conversation. If the interviewee brought up a lot of chal-
lenges by themselves it was first added late in the interview.
Development of overview
The interviews and barrier analysis were conducted simultaneously due to time con-
straints. The initial barrier themes, established at the beginning of Phase 5., were used to
develop an overview. Each box included a theme headline, keywords, and 2–3 examples
to make them more relatable.

CE strategies

• Short- and long-term agenda. Short-term is prof of concept (pilot project). Long-

Collaboration/Network:
• Material collaboration to source circular.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition

==PAGE:172==
• Take-back program. With a combination of many partners to solve it (Data, transport,

• Collaboration with an orchestrator with a portfolio of companies. Simplify the con-
tact, resource used and collaboration. Brings different capabilities: Data, Design

make it at scale.
Orchestrator: Is a company with value proposition of orchestrating.
• Convener in ISO and IEC to try update the standards to minimize the blocks they

Dynamics and challenges
• Distribution and adjustment of sustainability to all the different roles in an R&D de-
partment. Different responsibilities: Mechanics, materials, safety, project, market-
ing. All have a little to do with sustainability but not 100.
• Develop tools and recommendation to help other departments make the right choices.
What parameters they can work with. There is work advocating sustainability to the
other departments e.g. training, understanding and awareness.
• Collecting data from sub supplier could be a challenge. Has to communicate the
need for data from supplier, why it is collected. Importance they understand the
reason of the collection, it is not as frightening as they think.
• Making other the departments understand the value of collected data and measuring
on the improvement. Took years before the value was understood by other places
in organization.
• Data is not always placed where you would like it as sustainability engineer.
• Develop through projects. Has to let it mature bit by bit.
• Internal work: What should we do? How do we work with our products?
• Understand customers and their needs when creating take-back. How are they
handling the emballage from the products. Understand their waste management
needs. How accessible is it? How can we change this with a partner.
• Develop the take-back program are the most challenging case. Big and difficult
challenge to solve. Developing and innovating something new. Nobody else in the
sector has done it yet.
• Assessment of Net-value creation and ownership. There is a collected risk and
investment in the partnership when innovating and developing something new. Who
owns the solution? What are the value of the solution. Do you want to own it? What
is in it for us? what is in it for you? when is the price right?
• Defining the role in partnerships. What roles should the company have and what
should the partner have. e.g. There are companies better at the take-back challenge
than us. We are not a recycler or value recover company in our center.
• Assessment and alignment of capabilities. Understand what capabilities there are
in the company and what a partner should bring. e.g. as a retailer the company
has direct customer contact with the users and can create collection point at EOL in
156
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:173==
a take-back program. However the company needs a partner for (data, transport,
recycling, etc.).
• Validation of competences. In collaboration there is a testing phase of the different
partners. What are there competences. The work in screening a new partner and
understand what the solution is. Tried many recycling partner who were lacking the
full solution and only able to deliver a small part of the solution.
• 3’rd party consultant house. Validation through a partner, who is unfamiliar with, or
do not understand the product and need to communicate it to the customer.
• Challenge to develope around the high niche requirements and standards. Med-
tech has high niche requirements and safety standards. Is convener in ISO and
IEC to try update the standards to minimize the blocks they create.
• Take-back geographically. A lot of test of different setups in different countries to
develop the right solution and network. Wast collection and treatment is a geo-
graphically local agenda up to a certain radius. The solution has to be where the
end-of-life product is collected.
• Scale of solution. Solution has to be scalable but what is the cost?
• Lack of space for all companies to develop their own solution. Not room for all to
make individual take-back systems. e.g. lack of space at collection point at the
customer.
• Strategy and commitment: Distribution of effort and resources. There are different
sustainability strategies. How much effort and resources should be put in the differ-
ent solutions and strategies. They can be competing solutions e.g. take-back and
biodegradable solutions. Return of investment: It has to be a business case.
• Focus can be different from company to company. They can be measured on dif-
ferent requirements and legislation.

7. ”It is somewhat embedded that we have quality management, and we have data

8. ”There will be data you don’t have access to. It could be a process running at

9. ”We are dependent on the people in the value chain. It could be a raw material
supplier, someone casting components for us, or ultimately even our customers.

10. ”Another thing we have is a take-back program. A partnership is also involved here,
and in reality, it requires a whole network of collaborators to make it successful. It
involves data, transportation, a recycler, and ultimately also a buyer of the plastic.”...
”At least the most challenging case, because it is something fundamentally new.”...
”The incentive here is that there are probably others who are better at this than us.
We are not essentially a waste management company.”
11. ”We have a collaboration with some who act as orchestrator for the rest. So, I try to
make it a bit easier, in a way, for us to manage the contract or partnerships. Then
we have someone with the right incentives to help us drive it forward. But again,
there are some interfaces where we haven’t fully outsourced it.”
12. ”We have direct contact with our customers. So, it has been more about identifying

we are probably not the best suited to manage. ”
13. ”In the short term, someone needs to take the lead and make it work... We are
willing to do that, and there is an advantage in solving a problem for the customer.
But we also know that if everyone ... just sets up a bin, we will eventually run out of
space ... So, we need to work towards a sector-wide solution.”
14. ”They (orchestrator) are some who wants to live on this solution... they want to
make recycling a success... i believe it is a company not like a extended producer
responsibility group.”
15. ”Entering into a completely new partnership—it could be with anyone. There is a
sort of testing phase... You have to test each other a bit — what can you do? What
are your competencies? But at the same time, in a way, you are already in the
process of co-creating a solution that doesn’t yet exist.”
16. ”There isn’t necessarily an existing system in place. So, there is also a certain level
of mutual risk or a shared investment along the way. Who owns the outcome? ...
What is the value for us? Do we have an interest in owning this system? What’s in
it for us? What’s in it for you? When is the price right?... what is net-value?”
17. ”I think, for example, this challenge is really a network solution. It involves an incred-
ible number of partners across countries. So, there is probably a strong incentive to
find someone who can drive scale and develop expertise in this area.” (orchestrator)
18. ”Waste management is quite a local agenda. There is a specific material that needs
to be sent somewhere, and there are clear cut-off points—up to a certain distance,
it is environmentally justifiable to transport it, but beyond that, it isn’t.”

Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
==PAGE:175==
19. ”How do we create a business case? What is the cost per product? ”We are in a
pilot phase now, but when we scale up—that’s important. Can we scale up without

20. ”I think it has actually been fairly smooth. It’s also a bit like—if you compare it to

with someone who can, once again, drive it at scale makes it relatively easy.” (pro-

21. ”There are some tasks that are core responsibilities for us. Then there are things
we cannot do on our own. It could be related to validation or third-party validation…
In such cases, you need to find a partner... where it’s somewhat embedded, for
example, in selling through a consultant...you do all the work, send data to someone
who doesn’t understand the product, who then tries to create a report that turns out
to be extremely expensive. Then they go directly to a customer and communicate
it to them, even though they don’t really know the customer either.”
22. ”We have gone through several potential recycling partners, and we’ve been talking
to many of them to understand what’s out there... There is definitely a screening pro-
cess when finding new partners and also understanding what the solution actually
is.”
23. ”We are trying to create some work on standards to make it easier... There are a lot of
small barriers that just make the process frustrating and more resource-intensive... I
work within ISO and IEC, which are international standardization bodies. I’ve acted
as a convener, which is somewhat like a project manager, to establish a working
group and promote the initiative... It can be a long and tough process.”
24. ” When we talk about strategy and prioritization within a company, you also have to
consider—how many eggs do I put into take-back and recycling? How much do I
invest in our bioplastics initiatives or our product line?... Circular economy efforts
have different focus areas, right? So which one is actually the most important to
start with? I imagine companies must weigh these decisions”
25. ”So we shouldn’t be choosing bioplastics—something biodegradable—while also
running a recycling program separately. Trying to recycle plastic in that setup would
not be smart. So, we have done some preliminary work to define a design strategy—
this is what we are pursuing, and these elements need to align. Then there are
certain agendas we can move forward on more quickly.”

• Different collaboration dynamics: With competitors who in the beginning are not

• Lack of experts to manage partnerships (orchestrators). So not everyone need to
learn it. It is a field on its own.
• Lack of systematic mechanisms to orchestrate partnership.
• Uncertainty in confidentiality and the perceived barrier of it. Lack of ways to avoid it
affecting the process and being seen as a barrier.
• LAck of knowledge sharing.
• High amount of sustained resources and competences. If lack of financial surplus
or long term resources it may result in the collaboration fading out or never getting
started. Lack of opportunity to collaborate for small or medium size companies.
• Lack of standardization in the industry. e.g. materials.
Quotes:
1. ”And this can become even more complicated if you have to collaborate with com-
petitors... It could be partnerships with those who are aligned on a shared mission.
But then there are also those who are used to not sharing anything at all—let alone
collaborating—who are suddenly expected to work together instead of competing
against each other. So, there are many different dynamics in partnerships.”
2. ”I think there are a lot of potential pitfalls,... — it can take an incredibly long time,
and sometimes, even after investing a lot of time, the issue still can’t be resolved.”
3. ”I primarily work internally with internal partnerships.”
4. ”In that way, a company like (company C) have both corporate-level strategic part-
nerships as well as very practical and highly technical partnerships.”
5. ”We have a plastics strategy focused on reducing plastic use, minimizing the impact
of the materials we use, and implementing a take-back system.”

==PAGE:182==
7. ”To scout for new emerging opportunities for innovation within the circular agenda
and material types. We can support this in various ways. On one hand, by acting as

on how the solution matures. Many may show interest in the beginning, and then
either the collaboration continues or partners go their separate ways. But in any

of partnerships—what you initially think it will be may evolve into something entirely

8. ”Take-back was, from the very beginning, based on the idea that we cannot suc-

means collaborating within our own industry to bring together competitors.”
9. ”Ellen MacArthur Foundation — we are a member of their foundation. Can it be

10. ”What’s special about the ... collaboration is that it is actually CEO-led. This means

11. ”It’s important for us to collaborate and that we find common solutions. Because
by working together, we can drive a larger agenda—toward e.g. against suppliers.
Around 95% or more of most companies’ total impact lies within the supply chain.
Even though we see ourselves as a large company, for many suppliers, we are just
a small and insignificant player. But when we unite as an industry, we often share
the same suppliers, and that allows us to exert greater pressure.”
12. ”It’s also worth considering whether there’s a need for more professional expertise
to manage these partnerships so that not everyone who needs to learn it. Because it
is a discipline in itself—there are even educational programs where people special-
ize in partnerships. I can say for sure that the people in our social sector, who are
masters of partnerships, have a completely different approach and expertise in ar-
eas like communication, contract drafting, resolving obstacles, delegation, legal as-
pects, and so on. So sometimes, not everyone needs to be able to do everything—
not even at the company level. It could very well be a service that companies could
outsource.”
13. ”Also because it requires a continuous resource commitment from the companies
involved in a partnership. It’s not something you just do for three months and then
it’s done. It requires the company to allocate resources over a long period of time.
So, it might make sense to outsource it—just like you would hire an accountant.”
14. (Take-back orchestration - C2) ”But he is the one with that role, and he has it because
it is a key success factor for the project to even continue. The project’s continuation

together, the project would have been shut down, because it’s not something we
can—or should—handle alone. It was a non-negotiable design criterion that this

the years, along with countless tough negotiations—especially on the legal side.”
15. (orchestrator role) ”Sometimes we take the lead, and other times we end up going

may be?”
21. (Barrier) ”Yes, and one could consider whether a third party could handle confidential
information in such cases. If we are looking at external facilitators for partnership
thinking, there could be a third party managing confidential data on behalf of both
companies. This way, they could act as an intermediary, finding common ground
without disclosing sensitive information to either company.”
22. (Barrier) ”Knowledge is often something companies are reluctant to share, but at
the same time, it is the key prerequisite for achieving truly great results.”
23. (Barrier) ”I would say it requires strength—it takes resources to make it happen.

is not enough. You need sustained resources and competencies to invest in it. And
that, in itself, can be a challenge. If a company doesn’t have a financial surplus but
could significantly benefit from collaborating with others, they might never get there
simply because they lack the opportunity to do so.”...”So, there could be support
schemes to help facilitate this. It could also mean placing more focus on startups
and how they can be supported.”

25. ”I think all of these aspects are valid. But I believe that business models are among

Type of interview:

Question focus:
1. What is your role?
2. What kind of CE strategies are you working with?
3. Do you have any partnerships or collaborations, and if so, why? What was the
reason for establishing them?
4. Does other companies in the value chain orchestrate the collaboration?
5. Have you experienced any challenges that have been taken up a significant amount
of focus?
6. Are there specific resources you have used?
7. You tell me that you have built trust. Have you used a specific strategy, or has it
developed naturally over time?
Key findings:
CE strategies
• Take-back as an industry solution with focus on reintroducing recycled plastic
Collaboration
• Stages for collaboration: Initial dialog meeting. Involve the whole VC. Create five
proof points to work on prof-of-concept (validation). Including industry partners.
Guideline in how to act in meetings (off limit topics).
• All collaboration need trust, belief in the project. Understand the actors placement
in relation to each other. Building through active listening, designing solutions for
the common good. Mindset of making collaboration work. Seeking compromise.
• Vertical value chain collaboration and Horizontal industry or sector collaboration (in-
cluding the competing factor).
• Economies of scale by collaborating with partners.
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
169
==PAGE:186==
• Orchestrating in Denmark, but individual company in another country with the role.
Solely taking the role to ensure success and not seen as part of long-term strategy.
• Geographically are the ecosystem readiness high in Denmark.
• The barrier overview could maybe be sequenced (how do you initiate? how do you
structure the discussion? How do you work towards a clear end goals?)
Challenges
• Legal concerns of bringing sector together ensuring the free market and not to have
cartel-formation.
• Uncertainties about competitors. (Guideline)
• The challenge of building trust ensuring trust in the project as well.
• Difficult of find recycling or value recovery partner with viable business case around
it and financial opportunities.
• The challenge of scaling the solution and ensuring it is fully sustainable in VC.
Quotes:
1. (Take-back) ”And to do it so efficiently that we can actually use the recycled plastic
to mold new components for our devices. That, in short, is the strategy we are
following now.”
2. ”We have many partnerships, and from day one—when we started in Denmark back
in 2020. We actually started with what we call a dialogue meeting, where we invited
all our value chain partners to participate.”
3. ”It is actually the whole value chain we have involved because the solutions like the
one we are working on her cannot and should not be developed alone. We see
this as a broad collaboration across the entire value chain, where everyone must
contribute to ensuring successful recycling.”
4. ”There are both horizontal collaborations within the value chain, as I just mentioned,
and then there are more sector-/industry-focused vertical collaborations. This is
where we are saying — we don’t see this as an area where we should compete. Of
course, we compete in the market when it comes to selling our products, but when
it comes to sustainability, we believe that collaboration is the way forward to tackle
the major challenges.”
5. ”With four companies, we entered the market together, creating a joint initiative to
solve this challenge collectively. We identified five proof points to work on, to demon-
strate that an industry-solution and collaboration are the right thing to do more than
individual companies going at it alone. And so far, we have been quite successful
in achieving this.”
6. ”And of course, with four partners sharing the costs, it also becomes more cost-
effective. That’s also a part of it - ensuring we can do this as efficiently as possible.
Our goal is to recover as much plastic as possible from patients while also doing it
in the most cost-effective way. When we first started, it was extremely expensive in
the first year.”
7. ”We can achieve economies of scale, doing it with our partners, making everything
more cohesive. That’s why we have concluded that our strategy moving forward is
centered around collaboration and it is collaboration in the wide sense — both within
170
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition

the value chain and even more across the sector. We need to bring more and more

with our partners. We have project groups in place to ensure that it’s not just about

you have established also functions effectively within our products. And our goal is
rather to step back and have a third-party solution handle it for us. This could, for

collaboration with a recycling partner.”
10. ”So, partnerships and strategic alliances are very important for making the circular
economy a reality. You can’t do it alone — or rather, you can, but it would require
very large budgets and extensive resources to make it happen. At the end of the
day, this isn’t something a single company should take on. It requires a value chain

11. ”One of the major challenges is when bringing competing companies together. We

12. ”Of course, when you first enter into a collaboration with direct competitors and so
