13. GUIDELINES: ”We have actually built a high level of trust, allowing us to have good
discussions on these matters. However, when it comes to anything related to mar-
keted products, competition in the market, and pricing, we do not discuss these top-
ics at all. We have clear guidelines in place for every meeting, ensuring that these
subjects are off-limits. Meeting agendas are set, and minutes are taken. Naturally,
there are important considerations when collaborating with direct competitors.”
14. ECOSYSTEM: ”Getting a recycling partner up and running in this field, which is
a relatively new area, has also been a challenge. Again, when it comes to value
recovery companies—how many are there, and can they build a viable business
case around this? That’s the reality of business—if there isn’t a strong business
case, if they don’t see a financial opportunity or a strategic investment, it won’t be
sustainable in the long run. That’s why it has been crucial for us to establish a close
and strategic partnership.”
15. TRUST: ”How do you build trust? That’s a big and important question. There hasn’t
necessarily been a strict strategy behind it, if I may put it that way, but there have
been some guidelines for what we have done. Of course, we have also had legal
discussions, but always with the mindset that it’s about making the collaboration
work rather than creating conflict, so the focus has been on finding solutions and
compromises. That might be one way to build trust—by always approaching situ-
ations with openness and a willingness to find common ground. We also prioritize
transparency, and I believe that’s another key factor in building trust. We don’t have
Mapping value chain collaboration opportunities for circular economy transition
171
==PAGE:188==
a specific written strategy for this. But it’s clear that when you are transparent in
your approach — not just about what is going well, but also about the challenges

17. TRUST: ”How do you create a strategic relationship in what you do, especially if

believe that actions speak louder than words — though what you say is, of course,
important. But ultimately, it is through actions that you gradually build more trust
and time into the relationship.”

25. POWER STRUCTURE: ”I think we need to be very mindful of power structures...If

many people face the same challenges. If 
you’re kind, offer a cup of coffee, and ask 
insightful questions, most people are willing to
help, I think.” (B. Q. 7)
”Sure, we can crunch some numbers 
ourselves, but there’s a lot of value in bringing 
in third parties. That way, we can use the data 
more actively and say—it’s not just
something we made up.” (B. Q. 8)
”What’s special about the ... collaboration is 
that it is actually CEO-led. This means that our
CEO sits at the table a couple of times a year 
alongside his peers. So, they
have brought top executives together to 
discuss and take ownership of the agenda we 
want to drive collectively.” (C1 Q. 10)
B
”Which conceptual framework should we align
with? There is something called ecodesign, 
and there is something called circular 
economy. There is a significant over-
lap between the two. But what we call things 
also matters in terms of how we communicate
and operationalize them. I think we will end up
using the circular economy
framework here—but more to emphasize that 
this is a choice we need to make.” (B. Q. 5)
 ”Take-back has mostly been at a service level. If a product breaks, we, of course, deliver a new one. We have never 
implemented take-back from a sustainability point
of view. But that is something very exciting and relatively high on our to-do list.” (B. Q. 12)
Pilot partners. In Denmark—or in the Nordics, 
since we are based here—I think it’s relatively 
easy for us. We already have a few lined up 
where there is potential.” ”It
has actually gone both ways. There have also 
been people who have reached out to us and 
said, ’We have an idea—couldn’t we do this?’” 
(B Q. 16)
”It’s important for us to collaborate and that 
we find common solutions. Because by 
working together, we can drive a larger 
agenda—toward e.g. against suppliers.
Around 95% or more of most companies’ total 
impact lies within the supply chain. Even 
though we see ourselves as a large company, 
for many suppliers, we are just
a small and insignificant player. But when we 
unite as an industry, we often share the same 
suppliers, and that allows us to exert greater 
pressure.”(C1 Q. 11)
B
 ”And in being a software-as-a-service 
company, there is inherently some circular 
potential—at least in theory—that we are not 
yet leveraging. That misalignment of
vision might mean that we need to gain more 
clarity on the fact that this potential is worth 
exploring.” (B. Q. 15)
”So maybe we end up with six focus areas—we don’t know yet. But it’s interesting how that seems to be a 
manageable number for most companies.” (B. Q. 24)
B
”We don’t really have any direct 
involvement—we are, in a way, disconnected 
from our material flows at both ends. We don’t 
deal with scrap or production because we
order a lot of components from our suppliers, 
send them out and then we no longer have any 
contact with them.” (B. Q. 23)
C1
"We have a plastics strategy focused on 
reducing plastic use, minimizing the impact of 
the materials we use, and implementing a 
take-back system" (C. Q. 5)
"When something new is started, you reach out to those already working in the field. So, learning is definitely built on 
top of existing knowledge. But new employees
also join, and sometimes we even get people who are experts in partnerships—so we learn from them as well. I 
believe, and I hope, that this is something we will
all get better at over time. I just don’t think there are necessarily all the systemic mechanisms in place to remove 
every barrier along the way." (C1 Q. 18)
"And this can become even more complicated
if you have to collaborate with competitors... It
could be partnerships with those who are 
aligned on a shared mission.
But then there are also those who are used to 
not sharing anything at all—let alone 
collaborating—who are suddenly expected to 
work together instead of competing
against each other. So, there are many 
different dynamics in partnerships.” (C1. Q. 1)
It’s also worth considering whether there’s a 
need for more professional expertise to 
manage these partnerships so that not 
everyone who needs to learn it. Because it
is a discipline in itself—there are even 
educational programs where people 
specialize in partnerships. I can say for sure 
that the people in our social sector, who are
masters of partnerships, have a completely 
different approach and expertise in areas like 
communication, contract drafting, resolving 
obstacles, delegation, legal as-
pects, and so on. So sometimes, not everyone 
needs to be able to do everything not even at 
the company level. It could very well be a 
service that companies could
outsource.” (C1 Q. 12)
"—we are a member of their foundation. Can it 
be called a partnership? Maybe not entirely in 
the traditional sense. But for us, it is a kind of
partnership because it is the way we think 
about the circular economy. We collaborate 
with them on how they can develop their 
program in ways that create value for
us. We also use many of their resources in our 
training and internal education. So, we engage 
with them frequently and have an ongoing 
exchange of ideas.” (C1 Q. 9)
"I think there are a lot of potential pitfalls,... — 
it can take an incredibly long time, and 
sometimes, even after investing a lot of time, 
the issue still can’t be resolved." (C1 Q. 2)
”In most areas, we are fierce competitors, but 
when it comes to environmental issues, it’s 
the only space where we collaborate.” (C1. Q. 
27)
"In our device development and innovation 
area, they launched an innovation challenge a 
few years ago to identify alternative plastic 
types or other innovative solutions. From 
there, partnerships can be established,... DTU 
can also be involved, supporting the 
maturation of a startup by testing on our 
machines, etc." (C1. Q. 6) 
C1
"Take-back was, from the very beginning, 
based on the idea that we cannot successfully 
carry out this project unless we turn it into an 
industry-wide solution. That
means collaborating within our own industry 
to bring together competitors" (C1 Q. 8)
”Imagine if there were a smooth process—so if two partners were interested, they could just call a company, buy 30 
hours of expert consultancy, and get a sharp
consultant who says, ’Here’s how we do it.’ That way, they would be much further along instead of having to dance 
around it themselves.” (C1 Q. 19)
"In that way, a company like (company C) 
have both corporate-level strategic 
partnerships as well as very practical and 
highly technical partnerships." (C1 Q. 4)
”Also because it requires a continuous 
resource commitment from the companies 
involved in a partnership. It’s not something 
you just do for three months and then
it’s done. It requires the company to allocate 
resources over a long period of time. So, it 
might make sense to outsource it—just like 
you would hire an accountant.” (C1. Q. 13)
”There is also no legislation supporting these 
initiatives. It’s something one might wish 
existed.” ... ”There are no real mechanisms in 
place to support it as such. It’s
just the hope that 2 plus 2 equals 5—or a 
mission-driven ambition to contribute to 
change.” (C1 Q. 16)
Yes, and one could consider whether a third 
party could handle confidential information in 
such cases. If we are looking at external 
facilitators for partnership
thinking, there could be a third party managing
confidential data on behalf of both companies.
This way, they could act as an intermediary, 
finding common ground
without disclosing sensitive information to 
either company.” (C1 Q. 22)
C1
”But our devices—most of them—are used for 
about a month and then discarded. If we could 
extend their lifespan, it would be a huge 
advantage.” (C1 Q. 26)
To scout for new emerging opportunities for 
innovation within the circular agenda and 
material types. We can support this in various 
ways. On one hand, by acting as
a case company, and on the other, by entering 
into closer partnerships depending on how the 
solution matures. Many may show interest in 
the beginning, and then
either the collaboration continues or partners 
go their separate ways. But in any case, we 
also play a role in maturing some of these 
initiatives. That’s the nature
of partnerships—what you initially think it will 
be may evolve into something entirely 
different.” (C1 Q. 7)
Take-back orchestration - C2) ”But he is the 
one with that role, and he has it because it is a 
key success factor for the project to even 
continue. The project’s continuation
depends on that discipline succeeding. If they 
hadn’t managed to bring the industry together,
the project would have been shut down, 
because it’s not something we
can—or should—handle alone. It was a non-
negotiable design criterion that this had to be 
an industry-wide solution.”...”It has required 
blood, sweat, and tears over
the years, along with countless tough 
negotiations—especially on the legal side.” 
(C1 Q. 14)
If, at the EU level or even beyond, certain 
things could be standardized—for example, 
something like Ø-pict chargers, which are 
fortunately becoming more aligned
than they were initially—it would be a step in 
the right direction. Standardization can help 
remove some roadblocks, and I believe that’s 
the way forward.” (C1 Q. 28)
C1
I think all of these aspects are valid. But I 
believe that business models are among the 
hardest things to change—at least for a large 
‘supertanker’ like us, which already
operates within an established business 
model. If we were to follow the Circularity 
playbook completely, we would have to 
change our entire business model. That’s a
huge challenge. But we are working on it.” (C1.
Q. 25)
In our device development and innovation 
area, they launched an innovation challenge a 
few years ago to identify alternative plastic 
types or other innovative solu-
tions. From there, partnerships can be 
established,... DTU can also be involved, 
supporting the maturation of a startup by 
testing on our machines, etc.” ... (C1 Q. 6)
”Sometimes we take the lead, and other times
we end up going with it.”... ”And other times, 
we might find ourselves in a situation where 
we opt out
of something because we don’t have the 
resources to take on a leading role. And if no 
one else has the resources to take the lead 
either, I do worry that some things
might fall in between chairs. It’s a huge 
challenge to overcome and orchestrate 
everything.” (C1 Q.15.)
C1
And again, we are dealing with knowledge-
sharing. Companies want to protect their own 
business. So, who builds these bridges, and 
who can actually see them?
What are the mechanisms that bring people 
together? There could be innovation hubs, that
step in to facilitate collaborations and create 
new partnerships.” (C1 Q. 24)
C2
(Take-back) ”And to do it so efficiently that we 
can actually use the recycled plastic to mold 
new components for our devices. That, in 
short, is the strategy we are
following now.” (C2 Q. 1)
We have many partnerships, and from day one—when we started in Denmark back in 2020. We actually started with 
what we call a dialogue meeting, where we invited
all our value chain partners to participate.” (C2 Q. 2)
”And of course, with four partners sharing the 
costs, it also becomes more cost-effective. 
That’s also a part of it - ensuring we can do this
as efficiently as possible.
Our goal is to recover as much plastic as 
possible from patients while also doing it in 
the most cost-effective way. When we first 
started, it was extremely expensive in
the first year.” (C2. Q6.)
It is actually the whole value chain we have 
involved because the solutions like the one we
are working on her cannot and should not be 
developed alone. We see
this as a broad collaboration across the entire 
value chain, where everyone must contribute 
to ensuring successful recycling.”(C2. Q. 3)
”In Denmark, we take on an orchestrating role,
but we do so in close collaboration with our 
partners. We have project groups in place to 
ensure that it’s not just about
what we do, but rather a joint effort. Our 
approach is ensuring that all partners are well 
represented in the communication. In 
[another country], there is an independent
company called... They are also supported by 
a consulting firm, which acts as the host. So, it
is them who handle the operations.”(C2 Q. 8)
”With four companies, we entered the market 
together, creating a joint initiative to solve this
challenge collectively. We identified five proof 
points to work on, to demon-
strate that an industry-solution and 
collaboration are the right thing to do more 
than individual companies going at it alone. 
And so far, we have been quite successful
in achieving this.” (C2 Q. 5)
”Scandinavia consists of countries where the 
sustainability agenda is thriving or well-
established. We have learned this through 
launching in seven
different countries, giving us a sense of 
readiness across different markets. We are 
present in ...to test where companies and 
industries are prepared to embrace these
initiatives.” (C2. Q. 27)
There are both horizontal collaborations within
the value chain, as I just mentioned, and then 
there are more sector-/industry-focused 
vertical collaborations. This is
where we are saying — we don’t see this as an 
area where we should compete. Of course, we
compete in the market when it comes to 
selling our products, but when
it comes to sustainability, we believe that 
collaboration is the way forward to tackle the 
major challenges.” (C2. Q4)
POWER STRUCTURE: ”I think we need to be 
very mindful of power structures. We are 
strong in Denmark because our headquarters 
is here, which naturally gives us
influence. But that power must be managed 
with humility.If you have power, you should 
always exercise it with humility—that’s how I 
see it, at least. But sometimes
you have to dose it of course to get the right 
setup. You don’t just have to bend over 
backwards. You also have to say this is the 
right thing to do based on our
experience. You should talk with data, and be 
convincing with data.” (C2 Q. 25)

followed by the more detailed pages of barriers. If there is a red word, it describes a
connection to another theme. This connection is made based on the description and
context of the barrier from the collection phase.

Challenge of standardizing the scaling phase and develop an

”We are also in a scaling phase. How do we standardize this? It’s 
one thing to do it in Denmark, but how can we scale this further?... 
the key elements needed for a systemic approach to scaling, and 
that’s exactly what we are working on now. How do we develop 
what we call an operating model.” (C2. Q 31)

Company C
Scaling solution for long-term implementation in the value chain 
remains a challenge.
”That’s the challenge we’re facing—how do you scale? It’s not an 
art to run a small pilot project in one or two cities. The real 
challenge is scaling it into something with a significant impact—
something that becomes an integrated part of the entire value 
chain. That’s what we are working on.” (C2 Q. 26)
Scaling solution for 
long-term 
implementation

important. Can we scale up without breaking the bank?” (A. Q. 19)

economy of scale.

Misaligned organizations when trying to develop one policy and

Company A
Mismatch of capacities or capabilities.
“The concept of selling waste as an input to another industry is 
easily conceptualized, but the more challenging step is in the 
implementation or the ‘seizing’ capabilities phase.”(Chari et al., 2022  
p. 2507)
Assessment and alignment of capabilities. Understand what 
capabilities there are in the company and what a partner should 
bring. Tried many recycling partner who were lacking the full solution 
and only able to deliver a small part of the solution. - A
